 CHAPTER 1

Kiona’s implant was a week behind on its translation patch, so the river’s data-stream wasn’t singing the old songs anymore—it was spitting error codes in a dead colonial syntax. The council’s new fiber-optic roots, pulsing beneath the bank, had rerouted the ancestral chatter into a raw, screaming feed of satellite debris and forgotten treaties. She could feel the wrongness in her teeth.

She could hear it, the old, wet chuckle under the ice, a sound like stones rolling in a deep pocket. It was the first thaw song, the one that came before the real break-up, when the ice just got porous and sly. It was the most dangerous time to cross. And she was late because the drone had come at dawn, a black, silent wasp hovering at her window, and she’d had to spend an hour calming her grandmother down from the shaking fear of it.

“Tł'ih,” her grandmother had whispered, the old word for ghost, her knuckles white on the tin coffee cup. “They send their tł'ih to watch us now.”

Kiona hadn’t corrected her. A ghost was as good a word as any for a thing that saw without eyes, that carried your face back to people you’d never meet. She’d just drawn the curtain, boiled more spruce tea, and waited until the thing lost interest and slid away over the skeletal tops of the birch trees. Now the sun was a cold coin past its peak, and she had fifteen klicks to go.

She adjusted the pack on her shoulders—a caribou hide frame lashed with sinew, holding three frozen whitefish and a pouch of rendered fat—and studied the ice. It wasn’t white anymore. It was the colour of a dirty sky, webbed with dark veins. Talking ice. She took her tł'ogh from her belt, the long, curved walking stick of moose antler, and tapped it ahead of her. The sound was wrong. Hollow. Hungry.

“Okay,” she said to the river. “Let’s have a conversation.”

Her first steps were a negotiation. The ice groaned, a low, floorboard complaint. She moved not in a line, but in the old pattern her uncle taught her: the ptarmigan walk, shifting weight with a quick, light roll, never letting your whole self commit. The tł'ogh probed, found a soft spot, and she veered left. The wind cut across the open channel, scouring her cheeks, carrying the smell of distant diesel and the closer, cleaner scent of frozen mud and dead willow.

Halfway across, the river spoke clearly.

Krrrr-ack.

Not under her. Ten meters downstream. A black seam opened in the grey skin, and water, blacker still, welled up and smoked in the air. Kiona froze, not in panic, but in calculation. The ice would be shearing along pressure ridges. The sound told her where the weakness was propagating. She was on an island of stability. For now.

She exhaled, her breath a brief ghost of her own. “Still talking,” she murmured. “I’m still listening.”

A high, mechanical whine cut through the wind.

She didn’t look up. Looking up was what they wanted. Acknowledgement. She kept her eyes on the ice, but her periphery caught it: a sleek, carbon-grey quadcopter, lower than the drone, armed with a package pod. A Re-supply Courier. It zipped past her, following the river’s course north, towards the Settlement.

That was new. The couriers usually stuck to the new ice-road grid. The river was for the people who remembered it. The whine faded, replaced by a deeper, more visceral vibration through the soles of her boots. Something big on the ice-road, a few valleys over. A hauler. Bringing what? Taking what?

The questions were a distraction. The ice demanded her whole mind. She pushed them down, found her rhythm again, and reached the far bank just as the sun dipped behind the ragged teeth of the mountains. The snow here was trampled, not by caribou or moose, but by the deep, cleated prints of crawler-treads. The scars were fresh, cutting right through a stand of young spruce, splintering them like bones.

She knelt, not touching the prints, just looking. In the centre of one tread-mark, something glinted. She fished it out with a gloved finger. A data-chip. Standard issue, Settlement logistics. She held it. It was warm. It had fallen recently. It held nothing for her, just strings of numbers that would mean inventory, tonnage, coordinates. A bead of sweat from some operator’s neck. It was also a tiny, perfect eye. It could be pinged. It could tell them where she was.

Kiona considered the river. She drew her arm back to throw it into the black, chewing water.

She stopped.

Her grandmother’s voice, dry as willow leaves: Nothing they make is useless. Everything can be turned.

She slipped the chip into a small, beaded pouch at her belt, the one that held her flint and a pinch of tobacco. It sat there, alien among the ancient things.

The trail from the river to the ridge was a memory, not a path. She followed the hump of land where the blueberries grew thick in summer, avoided the sinkhole where the old bear denned, and climbed the last rocky scramble as the stars began to prick the violet dusk. At the top, the wind died.

Below, in the long valley, the Settlement burned.

Not with fire. With light. A cold, blue-white grid of it, laid out in a perfect, brutal rectangle against the dark land. Sodium arcs turned night into a shadowless, buzzing day over the compound. She could see the blocky shapes of the modular units, the landing pad, the skeletal tower with its blinking red eye. And on the far side, the new excavation: a raw, yellow gash in the earth, lit by banks of floodlights so harsh they seemed to peel the skin off the world. Even from here, she could hear the grind of machinery, a constant, ulcerating hum.

And beside it, almost hidden in the long shadow of the excavation, was the reason for her visit. A darker patch. The last stand of old-growth spruce in the valley, a tight knot of shadow and resilience. Her uncle’s cabin was a smudge at its edge.

That’s where the meeting was. The one she was now very late for.

She descended into the valley, leaving the clean cold of the ridge for the Settlement’s electronic twilight. The air changed. It carried a metallic tang, the smell of crushed rock and ozone. The snow here was grey with dust. She moved from the cover of one scraggly clump of alder to the next, a shadow among shadows. This was the fringe, where the wired world frayed into the old one.

A proximity sensor chirped from a post topped with a swirling blue light. She dropped flat behind a snowdrift. A recorded voice, genderless and calm, floated on the frozen air. “Sector Seven is active. Unauthorized personnel please proceed to the main gate for screening.”

She waited. The light swivelled, bored, and moved on. They were lazy with their perimeter here. They thought the land itself was the barrier. The cold, the distance, the river. They didn’t understand that the land was a road, for those who knew how to walk it.

She circled wide, coming at the spruce grove from the north, from the blind side of the excavation’s glare. The trees rose before her, a sudden wall of stillness. The noise of the machinery faded, absorbed by a million needles. The air softened, smelling of resin and deep, frozen duff. She stepped under the canopy, and the Settlement’s light became a distant, blasphemous glow.

Her uncle’s cabin was not a cabin. It was a convergence. The lower logs were hand-hewn, chinked with moss and clay. The middle section was patched with sheets of scavenged polymer and hull-plating from an old crawler. The roof was layered sod over a membrane of parachute silk. A satellite dish, gutted and rewired, hung from the eaves like a strange metal flower, feeding into a jury-rigged array of car batteries. Smoke, real wood smoke, curled from a stovepipe.

She didn’t knock. The door opened as she reached for it.

Her uncle, Tom, filled the doorway. He was a big man, his face a topography of scars and laugh lines. He wore a faded flannel shirt over a high-tech thermal base layer, caribou hide pants, and mukluks soled with grippy polymer tread from a Re-supply crate.

“The river talk you into a nap?” he grumbled, but his eyes were sharp, checking her over, seeing she was whole.

“A tł'ih paid a visit. Grandmother needed settling.”

He nodded, the one word explaining everything. “Get in. You’re the last.”

The inside was warm, close, and thick with the smell of stew, tobacco, and hot electronics. A dozen people were crowded in, sitting on stools, buckets, the bed frame. Kiona knew them all. Silas, with his hands permanently stained with motor grease. Annie, her hair in a long grey braid, sharpening a ulu knife on a stone. The young ones, Maya and Ben, faces tense with a mix of fear and excitement. They were trappers, hunters, mechanics, the ones who still lived out on the land, in the cracks of the Settlement’s footprint.

All eyes were on the center of the room. On the table.

On the thing growing there.

It was a root. A fat, gnarled taproot from a spruce, washed clean. But it wasn’t just lying there. It was wired. Fine filaments of copper, scavenged from god-knows-where, were wound around it in intricate patterns, connecting to a small solar cell, to a hand-crank generator, to a speaker cobbled together from a headset and a tin can.

Annie saw Kiona looking. “Tom’s latest madness,” she said, but her voice held no mockery.

“It’s not madness,” Tom said, squeezing past Kiona to stand by the table. He placed a hand on the root. “It’s a radio. But not for their voices. For the land’s.”

He nodded to Ben, who nervously took the hand-crank and began to turn it. A low, rhythmic thrum filled the room. The solar cell’s meter flickered. Tom adjusted a wire.

From the speaker, a sound emerged.

It was a hum. A deep, resonant, polyphonic hum. It had rhythm, but not a human one. It pulsed, swelled, faded, like a slow breath. Underneath it were clicks, pops, the faintest suggestion of running water.

“What… is it?” Maya whispered.

“It’s the groundwater,” Tom said, his voice reverent. “The electromagnetic pulse of the water moving through the rock, under the permafrost. The root feels it. The tree feels it. Always has. We just never had a way to hear it before they,” he jerked his head towards the Settlement’s glow, “started filling the world with noise and wires. This…” he tapped the root, “this listens under their noise.”

Kiona felt the sound in her teeth. It was ancient. It was the valley’s heartbeat.

“Why show us this, Tom?” Silas asked, his voice practical. “It’s a wonder. But we didn’t come here for wonders. The diggers are two hundred meters from the burial ridge. They’ve marked the spruce grove here for ‘biomass clearance’ next week. They’re not listening to groundwater. They’re listening for ore.”

Tom’s face hardened. “This is why.” He stopped the crank. The hum died, leaving the room feeling empty, thin. “They map the land with lasers and sonar. They see rock, density, profit. We need to map it with this.” He held up the wired root. “We need to show them the land isn’t empty. That it’s alive. On its own terms. In a language their instruments are deaf to.”

“They’ll say it’s static,” Ben said, discouraged. “They’ll say it’s a trick.”

“Then we make it a truth they can’t ignore,” Kiona said. Her voice was quiet, but it cut through the doubt. All heads turned to her. She was the youngest adult there, but she had her grandmother’s way of speaking—few words, each one placed with care. She pulled the warm data-chip from her pouch, held it up. It caught the lamplight. “They drop these. They’re everywhere. Their whole world runs on them. What if their machines started hearing our world? What if their maps started singing our songs?”

A slow smile spread across Tom’s face. It was a dangerous smile. “The chip. You can get it into their system?”

“I know a way,” Kiona said. She thought of the Re-supply Courier, following the river. A vein into the heart. “But the song… it needs to be more than groundwater. It needs to be a story. One that gets under their skin.”

Annie put down her ulu. “The old ones said the burial ridge wasn’t just where we put our dead. It was where the land remembers for us. Where the stories sink into the stone.”

Tom looked from the root, to the chip, to the faces around him. The plan was not a plan yet. It was a feeling, a direction, like following a caribou trail in fresh snow. “Then we go to the ridge,” he said. “Before their machines do. We ask the land for a story. And we give it a voice loud enough to break their world.”

Outside, a siren wailed from the Settlement, a short, sharp blast marking a shift change. The meeting was over. They left one by one, slipping back into the trees. Kiona was the last.

Tom handed her a small, wrapped package. “For your grandmother. Dried mint from the south slope.”

She took it. “The root radio… does it have a name?”

He looked at the tangled, beautiful mess of wood and wire. “I call it the Throat,” he said. “Everything needs a throat to speak.”

Kiona stepped back into the night. The Throat. She liked it. She looked at the glaring wound of the excavation, then up at the stars, sharp and clear above the spruce. The river would be quieter now, the ice settling into its nightly silence. She had a longer, darker walk home. And a new kind of listening to do.

She touched the data-chip in her pouch. It was no longer just a piece of their world. It was a seed. Now they had to plant it in the belly of the beast, and see what strange, wild fruit would grow.

 CHAPTER 2

The mint was for tea, but the smell of it, sharp and green, was for memory. Kiona’s grandmother held the dried leaves to her nose, eyes closed, and for a moment she wasn’t in the single-room cabin with its patched walls and the constant, low-grade fear. She was on the south slope in late summer, the sun warm on her back, the air humming with bees.

“He remembers,” she said, her voice a rustle. “Your uncle. He remembers the places.”

She crumbled the leaves into a pot of hot water. The scent bloomed, fighting the smell of damp wool and cold earth that always seeped in through the floorboards. The dawn light, weak and grey, did little to push back the dark in the corners. The drone had not returned. The silence felt like a held breath.

Kiona was repairing a snowshoe, replacing a broken crosspiece with a strip of steamed birch. Her hands worked on their own, a familiar rhythm of pull and tie. Her mind was on the ridge.

“You’re going to the old place,” her grandmother said. It wasn’t a question. She had a way of listening to the spaces between words.

“Tom thinks the land has a story for us. One we can use.”

“Use.” The old woman tasted the word, found it bitter. “The land’s stories are not tools. They are truths. You don’t use a truth. You carry it. Or it crushes you.”

“They’re going to dig it up, Shǫǫ.” Kiona rarely used the word for grandmother. It was for when she needed the weight of it. “They’ll take the stones, grind them for their roads. The stories will be dust in their treads.”

Her grandmother poured the tea into two chipped enamel cups. She slid one across the table to Kiona. “And you think giving the land a new kind of voice will stop them? You think they understand any language but the one of taking?”

“No,” Kiona said, honestly. “But maybe we can make their machines speak in tongues. Maybe we can confuse the taking.”

A faint, dry sound escaped her grandmother. It might have been a laugh. “Like putting a grass snake in the crawler’s fuel line. It won’t stop it, but the driver will remember the fuss.” She sipped her tea. “The ridge… it is a careful place. The stories there are not just in the stones. They are in the quiet between them. Your uncle’s wire-throat… it might hear the stones. But will it hear the quiet?”

Kiona had no answer. She finished lashing the snowshoe, tested its tension. It was good. Strong. A tool for walking softly.

“I will come with you,” her grandmother said.

Kiona looked up, startled. “It’s a long walk. The ice—”

“I have walked that ridge since before your mother was a spark in my belly. I know its ice. I know its silence. And if you are going to ask the land for a gift, you do not send a child alone. You bring an elder. For respect.”

There was no arguing. When her grandmother spoke in that tone, it was the earth itself speaking. Kiona just nodded.

They left when the light was still the colour of a bruise, following a different river trail, one that wound up into the foothills away from the Settlement’s sightlines. Her grandmother moved slowly, but with a relentless, ground-eating pace. She used no walking stick, but her hands, when they brushed a tree trunk or scooped a handful of snow to suck on, were like maps, reading the texture of bark, the granularity of the snow.

They walked in a companionable silence for hours, the only sounds their breath, the crunch of snow, and the distant, ever-present thrum of the Settlement’s generators, a sound so constant it had become part of the weather.

Near midday, they stopped by a spring that bubbled up, miraculously unfrozen, from a cleft in a rock. They ate strips of dried caribou and cold bannock. Her grandmother pointed with her chin to a scar on a nearby cliff face. “See that? Not a blast mark. That’s from the ice river, ten thousand years ago. It flowed here. It left its signature. The Settlement’s maps call this ‘Inert Geologic Feature 7-B.’” She spat into the snow. “Inert. A word for dead things.”

“Tom’s Throat heard the groundwater,” Kiona said. “It’s not inert.”

“Water moves. Water has a voice. Stone…” her grandmother trailed off, looking at the cliff. “Stone has memory. A slower memory. You need a different kind of ear.”

As they climbed higher, the trees thinned, and the wind picked up, scouring the snow into hard, sculpted waves. The Settlement was a geometric smudge in the vast, white bowl of the valley below. The excavation was a raw, yellow thumbprint. From here, Kiona could see the tiny, ant-like movement of the haulers. Systematic. Relentless.

Her grandmother stopped, her hand on Kiona’s arm. Her eyes were fixed not on the Settlement, but on the sky to the north. A line of dark clouds was massing, but that wasn’t it. A flicker of movement, too fast for a bird, too silent for a plane.

“Another tł'ih?” Kiona asked.

“No. Bigger. A… ǫǫ̀.” She used the word for a large, predatory bird. “A metal one.”

It was a surveillance craft, a sleek, winged thing that glided on some near-silent propulsion. It banked in a wide, lazy arc over the valley, then turned its nose towards the ridge. Towards them.

“Down,” Kiona hissed, pulling her grandmother behind a tumble of boulders.

They crouched in the lee of the rock. The craft’s shadow slid over the snow, a swift, dark stain. It didn’t slow. It didn’t hover. It passed directly overhead, and for a second, Kiona imagined she could feel the vibration of its cameras, a predatory attention focused on the rocks, the snow, the two heat-signatures hiding below.

Then it was gone, continuing its patrol.

“They’re watching the high places now,” her grandmother said, her voice grim. “They know the land. They are learning to look at it.”

The encounter cast a pall over the rest of the climb. The easy silence was gone, replaced by a watchful tension. Every shadow felt like a lens. Every gust of wind sounded like a rotor.

They reached the burial ridge as the short day began to fail.

It was not dramatic. Not a cliff or a pinnacle. It was a long, gentle backbone of land, dotted with clusters of lichen-crusted stones. Some were placed in circles. Some stood alone, leaning with the centuries of wind. There were no bodies, not anymore. The permafrost and the time had taken them. What remained was the intention. The memory of placement.

Her grandmother walked to the centre of the largest circle. She didn’t enter it. She stood at its edge, bowed her head, and spoke softly in the old language. Greetings. Names. An apology for the intrusion.

Kiona stood back, feeling like an eavesdropper. The wind here had a different sound. It whistled through the stone arrangements with a peculiar, melodic tone, like a flute played by a giant. The stone memory.

Her grandmother finished and gestured Kiona forward. “Now. You have your listening thing?”

Kiona unpacked a smaller version of Tom’s device from her pack. A spruce root, thinner, wired to a compact battery and a solid-state recorder Tom had salvaged and “de-branded.” It was less a radio, more a stethoscope.

“Where?” Kiona asked.

Her grandmother closed her eyes, turned her face into the wind. She pointed to a low, flat stone, half-buried in the snow at the heart of the circle. “There. That one is a heart-stone. It was placed when my grandmother’s grandmother was a girl. It holds a story of a great migration, when the caribou changed their path.”

Kiona knelt, brushed the snow away. The stone was dark, almost black, shot through with veins of quartz that glittered in the low light. She carefully wound the wire filaments around it, making contact. She switched on the recorder. The battery hummed.

At first, there was nothing. Just the hiss of the wind in the microphone.

Then, a vibration. A deep, sub-audible thrum that made the stone tremble under her fingertips. It wasn’t the pulse of water. This was slower. Heavier. A tectonic rhythm.

She turned up the gain.

The speaker emitted a low, grinding drone. It was an uncomfortable sound. It felt like pressure in the bones. Under the drone, faintly, were other textures: the skitter of a billion crystalline structures shifting infinitesimally, the sigh of continents resting on a molten heart.

“It’s… the stone’s memory?” Kiona whispered, awed and unsettled.

“Play it back,” her grandmother said.

Kiona rewound a few seconds and hit play. The grinding drone filled the air around the stone circle.

And the wind changed.

It wasn’t her imagination. The steady flute-whistle through the stones shifted pitch. It rose, harmonizing with the recorded drone, then clashing against it. The sound became a dissonant wail that seemed to come from everywhere at once.

A small stone, no bigger than a fist, perched on top of a larger one, trembled and fell into the snow with a soft thump.

Her grandmother’s face was pale. “Stop it.”

Kiona fumbled, switched the recorder off. The artificial drone died. The wind’s wail subsided, slowly returning to its normal mournful flute tone.

They stood in the sudden, ringing silence. The fallen stone was an accusation in the snow.

“It’s not a memory,” her grandmother said, her voice hushed with horror. “It is a disturbance. You are not listening to a story. You are shouting into a deep, dark well with a metal voice, and the echo… the echo moves things that should not be moved.” She looked at the wired root with disgust. “This is not a Throat. This is a shout. And you will bring the whole ridge down on our heads.”

Kiona stared at the device. She had thought they were offering the land a microphone. Instead, they had given it a feedback screech. A violent, unnatural vibration. Her grandmother was right. They were poking the stone memory with a stick.

But as she looked from the fallen stone to the distant, glowing sore of the excavation, a cold, hard thought crystallized.

What if a shout was what they needed?

Not a story to be understood. A vibration to disrupt.

“Shǫǫ,” Kiona said slowly, picking up the fallen stone and placing it gently back. It felt warm. “What if… we’re not trying to tell them a story? What if we’re trying to give the land a weapon? A shout loud enough to crack their listening posts? To make their machines tremble on their foundations?”

Her grandmother looked at her as if seeing a stranger. “You would weaponize a heartbeat? You would turn a memory into a bomb?”

“They are turning a memory into a road!” Kiona’s voice broke, surprising herself with its ferocity. “They are turning the south slope into a data-farm! They are turning your memories into ‘inert features’! If the land’s own voice, its own deep vibration, is the only thing they might feel… is that not a form of respect? To fight back with the land’s own strength?”

The old woman was silent for a long time. The wind fluted. The stone under Kiona’s wires felt alive, pregnant with a terrible power they had just tickled.

“It is a dangerous prayer,” her grandmother said finally. Her eyes were on the Settlement’s lights, winking on one by one in the twilight. “To ask the land to fight for you. You must be sure you are fighting for the land. And you must be ready for the kind of fight the land wages. It is not quick. It is not clean. It is slow, and cold, and it grinds stone to dust.” She turned and began to walk back down the ridge. “Bring your shouting stick. We will talk to your uncle. But know this, granddaughter: you have heard the stone’s voice now. And it is not a song. It is a warning.”

Kiona packed up the device, her hands trembling. The data-chip in her pouch felt heavier than ever. They had come for a story and found a seismic charge. As she followed her grandmother’s tracks down the darkening slope, the grinding drone still seemed to echo in her skull, a new and terrible rhythm. The land wasn’t just speaking.

It was growling.

 CHAPTER 3

Tom’s cabin smelled of solder and dread.

The wired root—the Throat—sat in the center of the table like a pagan altar. Kiona’s smaller recorder played the stone-drone on a loop, its low, grinding frequency making the tin cups on the shelf rattle in sympathetic vibration. Annie, Silas, Maya, and Ben listened, their faces drawn tight. Kiona’s grandmother sat by the stove, her back to the room, drinking tea and staring into the fire as if the answers were in the flames.

When the recording finished, the silence was thick.

“It’s not a broadcast,” Silas said finally, rubbing his greasy hands on his pants. “It’s a… a tremor. You can’t put that on a data-chip. It’ll corrupt everything around it. It’s noise.”

“That’s the point,” Kiona said. She felt charged, electric from the ridge. “We don’t send a message. We send a sickness. We inject their system with the land’s own indigestion.”

Tom was staring at the Throat, his big hands resting on the table. “The chip you have… it’s a logistics tag. It gets scanned at the gate, at the warehouse, at the dig site. It talks to their network. If we could make it emit this…” he gestured to the recorder, “…as a carrier wave, a background shiver…”

“It would be like a ghost in the wires,” Ben said, a flicker of understanding in his eyes. “Their sensors would get jumpy. Their comms would have static. The diggers’ guidance systems might… hiccup.”

“More than a hiccup,” Tom said. “If we can tune it right, match it to the resonant frequency of their machinery… we could make their world itch. Make the ground under their crawlers feel unstable. Make their lights flicker with a rhythm that isn’t on the grid.”

“And then they leave?” Maya asked, hope thin in her voice.

“No,” Kiona’s grandmother said from the stove, not turning around. “Then they look harder. They send more tł'ih. They bring machines that dig deeper, listen harder. A shout does not drive away a bear. It tells the bear where you are.”

“So we don’t just shout once,” Kiona said, the plan forming as she spoke, cold and clear. “We make the land itself seem… pathological. Un-cooperative. We make their technology fail when it points at the sacred places. We make the burial ridge a dead zone for their instruments. We make the south slope give their drones vertigo. We don’t fight them. We make the land fight for itself, through their own tools.”

It was a maximalist thought. Not hiding, but amplifying. Not preserving a silent truth, but weaponizing a loud, inconvenient one.

Tom’s eyes gleamed. “We’d need more than one chip. We’d need a chorus. And we’d need to tune each one to a different place—the ridge, the south slope, the river bend. A symphony of interference.”

“And how do we get these tuned chips into their bloodstream?” Annie asked, practical as ever. “We can’t just walk up to the gate.”

Kiona thought of the Re-supply Courier, the wasp-like drone. “The courier that follows the river. It lands on a pad at the logistics hub to offload. It’s not heavily guarded. It’s just a machine. If we could intercept it, just for a minute, swap out a data-pallet’s chip for one of ours…”

“That’s a five-minute window, max,” Silas said. “And you’d need to be within ten meters to hack its hatch with a manual override. That’s deep in their sensor field.”

“The river,” Kiona said. “During the spring melt, the bank under the landing pad erodes. They have a temporary sensor blind-spot there. I’ve seen it. The ice talks, and their wires get confused by the run-off.”

A slow, collective breath was drawn around the table. It was possible. It was also insane.

“We’d have one shot,” Tom said. “The first chip that goes active will alert them. The whole system will go on alert. We need all the chips to sing at once. A cacophony.”

They worked through the night. Tom and Ben de-soldered a dozen data-chips from broken equipment Silas had scavenged from the dump. Kiona and Annie worked with the Throat, using the recording from the ridge as a base frequency, then modulating it—slowing it down to a deep, gut-churning throb for the burial ridge, speeding it up to a high-pitched, nerve-jangling whine for the south slope where the mint grew, adding the watery pulse of the river to a third. Each chip was programmed with a simple, corrupting loop of sound-as-data.

By dawn, they had twelve chips. Twelve tiny shouts in plastic shells.

Kiona’s grandmother had not moved from the stove. As the others packed the chips into a waterproof case, she stood up, her joints protesting. She walked to the table and picked up one of the chips. It looked so insignificant in her wrinkled palm.

“This is your prayer,” she said to Kiona, her voice tired. “A prayer of interference. Of static. I will not bless it. But I will tell you this: the land does not know mercy. If you ask it to fight, it will not distinguish between their machines and your feet. The tremor you start will not stay where you put it. You are waking something up. Be ready for what answers.”

She placed the chip back on the table and left, slipping out into the grey pre-dawn without a sound.

Her words hung in the close air. A final, cold truth.

Tom broke the silence. “The main melt starts in three days. The forecast calls for a warm wind. That’s our window.”

The plan was set. They dispersed into the creeping light, each with a task. Silas and Ben would plant chips near key infrastructure using their knowledge of the maintenance tunnels. Maya would watch the patrol patterns. Annie would be ready at the cabin with a fallback location.

Kiona and Tom would handle the courier.

For the next two days, Kiona moved through a world of heightened sensation. Every sound from the Settlement was a potential alarm. Every drone in the sky was a sentry. She checked the river ice a dozen times a day, listening to its conversation turn from sly chuckle to wet, rushing roar. The thaw was coming early, accelerated by the Settlement’s heat-island. Even the weather was being bent.

The night before the melt, she couldn’t sleep. She lay on her bunk, listening to her grandmother’s steady breathing from across the room. The old woman had not spoken of the plan again. Her silence was a wall.

Just before dawn, Kiona heard it. A new sound, weaving with the river’s voice. A deep, rhythmic chug-chug-chug that vibrated through the ground. She went to the window.

In the valley, under the flat glare of the arc-lights, a new machine had arrived. It was colossal. A walking excavator, with four hydraulic legs like a giant insect, and a boom-arm that could reach five stories high. It was being assembled on the edge of the yellow gash, its silhouette a jagged blasphemy against the starry sky. Its purpose was clear: to clear the spruce grove. To take the trees in great, greedy handfuls.

Their window had just shrunk.

She dressed quickly in the dark, her hands steady. She packed the case with the chips. She checked her tools: the manual override probe, the insulated cutters, the bone-handled knife. When she turned, her grandmother was sitting up on her cot, watching her.

“The big machine is here,” Kiona said.

“I heard its heart,” her grandmother replied. “It is a hungry heart.”

Kiona knelt by her cot. “I have to go.”

The old woman reached out, her hand cool and dry on Kiona’s cheek. It was a gesture from childhood. “You carry our voice into the metal world. Do not forget which voice is yours.” She pressed something into Kiona’s hand. A small, woven bag of muskox wool. “Tobacco. For the river. When you ask a spirit to help you, you must give it something first.”

Kiona clutched the bag, a lump in her throat. She nodded, unable to speak, and slipped out the door.

The world outside was a monochrome of dark blue and black. She met Tom at the edge of the spruce grove. He had seen the walking excavator too. His face was grim.

“Change of plan,” he whispered. “We do it tonight. Now. Before that thing is operational. The melt has started early. The river’s loud. It will cover us.”

They moved like ghosts through the fringe, using the machinery’s own noise as cover. The chug-chug-chug of the walking excavator’s generators was a perfect drumbeat for their infiltration. They reached the eroded bank below the logistics landing pad. The soil was wet and unstable, the river gnawing at it hungrily. Just as Kiona had predicted, the sensor post on the bank listed at a crazy angle, its light dark.

Above them, on

---

 Chapter 4

 CHAPTER 4

The landing pad was a scar of poured light above them, a chemical yellow that did not belong to the night. It bleached the snow beneath it into a dead, flat sheet. From their pocket of shadow under the bank, Kiona and Tom watched the silhouettes of two security techs move across that brightness, their breath pluming in the false day. The chug-chug-chug of the walking excavator’s generators was a constant, gut-deep tremor through the ground, a rhythm that made Kiona’s teeth ache.

Tom pointed a gloved finger, tracing a path along the eroded bank. The river had done half their work. The sensor post leaned drunkenly, its housing cracked open like an egg, wires spilling into the mud. The collapse had taken a bite out of the bank directly beneath the pad’s western support pylon. The pylon itself, a thick trunk of composite and steel, was exposed down to its foundations, which were not foundations at all, but anchors drilled into permafrost that was no longer permanent. The river was talking to it, too. A low, slushing sound of dissolution.

“There,” Tom mouthed.

The plan was a fragile thing, spun from desperation and the river’s own hunger. Infiltrate the pad’s underbelly through the erosion gap. Access the main data conduit junction where it ran down the compromised pylon. Upload the chips—the recorded river songs, the ground-penetrating radar maps of the ancient fish runs, the elders’ testimonies that were not testimonies but stories, the kind that got under the skin of law. A data-poem. A ghost in the machine. It was their last, best voice.

Kiona checked the case at her hip, felt the solid click of the latches. Her grandmother’s tobacco pouch was a warm lump against her chest. She thought of the hungry heart of the machine, and her own heart beat a frantic counter-rhythm against it.

One of the techs on the pad lit a cigarette, the flare of the match a tiny, human punctuation in the industrial glare. The other laughed, a sound swallowed by the generator’s drone. They walked toward the far side, drawn by the spectacle of the assembling excavator.

“Go,” Tom said.

They went up the bank like otters, low and fluid, using the frozen clods and exposed roots for purchase. The mud was a cold, sucking grip at their boots. They reached the shadow of the pylon, a world of stark black and dripping ice. The junction box was there, a sealed grey cube mounted at chest-height, lit by a single, faltering status LED. Kiona unslung her pack, her fingers already pulling out the manual override probe. Tom kept watch, his body a tense column against the blue dark.

The probe slid into the maintenance port. She felt the internal locks resist, then yield with a soft, metallic sigh. The casing unsealed. Inside, a nest of fiber-optic threads glowed with faint, racing colors—data like blood in capillaries. The central processor block was cool to the touch. This was the artery. This was where they had to speak.

She opened the case. The chips were slivers of obsidian, inert and heavy. She slotted the first one home. A progress bar, invisible to her, would be filling on some server a thousand miles away. A ghost entering the house.

A new sound cut through the generator’s beat. Not the river. A harmonic, rising whine. Then a heavy, rhythmic crunch-crunch-crunch.

Tom’s hand clamped on her shoulder. He didn’t need to point.

Up on the pad, backlit by the lights, the walking excavator took its first step.

It was a motion that defied sense. The great insectile leg, twice as thick as a spruce trunk, pistoned upward, hinged, and then plunged downward, the pointed foot sinking deep into the gravel and frozen earth. The entire machine listed, then stabilized. The boom-arm, a skeletal giant’s finger, swung lazily in a test arc. The crunch was the sound of the world being broken deliberately.

Kiona’s breath caught. The plan, the fragile plan, had assumed the machine was still asleep. It was not. It was stretching.

“Faster,” Tom whispered, his voice stripped thin.

She slotted the second chip. The river’s thaw song, digitized, a whisper of ancient water into a stream of ones and zeroes. The third chip. The fish runs, the memory of silver bodies in the dark.

Crunch-crunch-crunch.
Another step. It was turning. Its floodlights, cold and surgical, swept across the far tree line, turning the spruce into stark, black cutouts.

Kiona’s fingers fumbled the fourth chip. It clattered against the casing. She snatched it from the air, pushed it home. The stories. The voice.

A different light hit them. Not the excavator’s. A white, searching beam from the eastern edge of the pad. A handheld spotlight.

“Hey!” A voice, human and sharp, cut through the mechanical noise. “Activity at Pylon Seven! Under the platform!”

The plan tore like rotten cloth.

Tom shoved her. “Go! Down to the river!”

“The upload’s not—”

“Go!”

He turned, not down the bank, but up, pulling himself onto the lip of the pad, drawing his body into a crouch. A diversion. A stupid, brave diversion.

Kiona slammed the junction box closed, the case in her hand, and half-slid, half-fell down the unstable bank. Mud and ice scraped against her parka. She hit the bottom and ran, not away, but along the bank toward the thicker darkness of the spruce grove. The spotlight beam jabbed down, searching, finding Tom.

“Stop right there! Hands where I can see them!”

She glanced back. Tom stood on the edge of the light, his hands raised slowly. He was looking not at the security guard, but past him, at the excavator. His mouth was open in a shape that was not fear, but awe.

Kiona followed his gaze.

The walking excavator, having turned, was now facing the grove. Its floodlights pinned the trees in a cage of light. The boom-arm lowered, not with the jerky motion of a machine, but with a terrible, predatory grace. At its end, the shears—massive, serrated blades meant for delimbing whole trees—opened like the maw of a metal lamprey.

It took another step toward the grove. The ground transmitted the impact up through Kiona’s bones.

This was not just clearance. This was a statement. A demonstration. The hungry heart was beating, and it was coming to eat.

She plunged into the trees. The world shut out the direct light, became a maze of blue-black trunks and the panicked scatter of her own breath. The case was a clumsy weight. She had to get it to the fallback, the old trapper’s cabin upriver. She had to finish the upload. It was the only voice they had left.

But the river was talking louder now, a rushing, gleeful sound. The first real melt was beginning. And between her and the cabin was the crossing. The sly, porous ice. The most dangerous time.

She ran, the sounds of shouting and the relentless crunch-crunch-crunch of the machine fading behind her, replaced by the wet, growing chuckle of the water. She was carrying a ghost in a case, and a pouch of tobacco for a spirit, and the two weights felt exactly the same.

The spruce grove was a cathedral she had known since childhood, but now it felt like a tomb waiting to be desecrated. The light from the excavator bled through the branches, creating long, moving shadows that tricked the eye. Every familiar stump, every twist in the game trail, became a potential ambush. She moved by feel more than sight, the memory in her muscles more reliable than her racing mind.

The river’s voice grew from a chuckle to a low, steady roar. It was the sound of weight shifting, of a billion crystalline bonds breaking. She reached the bank at the old crossing point. The ice looked the same—a white highway under the starlight. But the sound was all wrong. It was a wet, grumbling exhalation.

From back toward the pad, a new sound joined the symphony: the shriek of rending wood. A sharp, singular crack, like a giant’s bone breaking, followed by a vast, sighing crash. Then the excavator’s engine revved, a sound of pure satisfaction. They were taking the first trees. The grove was bleeding.

Kiona’s throat tightened. She forced her eyes away from the distant, hellish glow and onto the ice. Tom’s words echoed. The river will cover us. It would cover her, too, if she misstepped. She unzipped her parka, fumbled for the muskox wool pouch. Her fingers were stiff. She pinched a generous offering of the dark, fragrant tobacco and knelt at the very edge of the bank.

“Old Mother,” she whispered, the words stolen by the roar. “I carry a ghost that needs to speak. Lend me your solid back.”

She sprinkled the tobacco onto the dark, open water at the bank’s edge. It swirled and vanished. No sign, no acknowledgment. The spirit was either busy, or indifferent, or waiting.

There was no more time. She stood, adjusted the strap of the case across her body, and stepped onto the ice.

It did not crack. It groaned. A deep, resonant complaint that traveled up her legs. She knew this language. This was the ice saying it was tired. She began the crossing, not in a straight line, but in the old, zig-zag path that followed the strongest flows. Each step was a negotiation. A testing of the surface with her weight before committing. The roar was all around her now, coming up through the ice as much as through the air. She was walking on the roof of a shouting cavern.

Halfway across, the ice changed. It became darker, translucent where the snow had blown away. She could see the movement beneath, the black water streaked with pale, suspended platelets of ice. It was like looking at the sky through a night storm.

A sound behind her. Not the river. A drone.

She froze, mid-step. The familiar, waspish whine. She turned her head slowly.

It was there, hovering at the tree line behind her, a black cutout against the lesser dark. Its single, red eye was lit. It had found her. It was watching her cross.

The knowledge was a cold spike in her gut. They knew. They knew she had the data. They would track her to the cabin. They would take the ghost before it could speak.

The ice groaned again, a longer, more plaintive sound. The drone advanced, gliding out over the bank, maintaining its distance but keeping her locked in its eye.

A new calculation, cold and sharp, cut through her fear. The cabin was compromised. The fallback was a trap. She had to change the destination. There was only one place left where a ghost might hide, where the metal world’s eyes might not follow.

She looked downriver, toward the deep canyon where the river narrowed and plunged between cliffs of basalt. The Raven’s Throat. The ice there would be thin, non-existent in the rapids. But the cliffs… there were old ways. Places to climb. Places to hide.

The decision was a physical shift inside her. She stopped her careful zig-zag. She turned directly downriver, toward the growing thunder, and began to run.

The ice protested violently. A spiderweb of cracks shot out from her boots, white lines on the dark surface. The drone’s whine pitched higher, accelerating to follow.

She ran not away from the roar, but into it. The smooth ice gave way to broken pressure ridges, heaves of ice that had buckled like pottery. She scrambled over them, the case banging against her ribs. The drone was closer now, its shadow flitting over the jagged landscape around her.

Ahead, the world ended. The flat river ice terminated in a jagged line of black. Beyond was the roar, unleashed. The canyon.

She reached the edge. Below, the river was a chaos of churning black water and car-sized ice chunks being tossed like gravel. The mist rose cold and stinging. The opposite cliff was a sheer, dark wall, slick with spray.

The drone hovered ten feet behind her, at the very edge of the stable ice. Its red eye watched, unblinking. Recording the fugitive at the precipice.

Kiona looked at it. She saw her own face, reflected a thousand times in some distant server. A ghost carried by a ghost.

She backed up two steps, took a breath that was half mist, and ran straight for the edge.

She did not jump into the water. At the last possible moment, she dropped to her knees, sliding on the wet ice, and grabbed for the one thing she had remembered—a rusted, ancient piton driven into the cliff face just below the rim, a relic from some long-ago climber. Her gloves scraped over wet rock, caught. Her body slammed against the cliff, her legs dangling over the furious void.

Above, the drone shot forward, peering over the edge. Its red eye gazed down at her, a silent question.

Kiona let go with one hand, swung wildly, and grabbed a narrow, icy ledge with her fingertips. She began to move sideways, crab-like, away from the piton, away from the drone’s direct view, into the thick curtain of mist. The world shrank to the inch of rock under her fingers, the scream of water below, and the fading, frustrated whine of the machine above.

She had asked the river for its solid back. It had given her a wet, crumbling wall instead. It was help of a kind. She clung there, in the thunder and the mist, the case heavy on her back. The ghost was still with her. But so were the eyes. And the hungry heart was still beating, back in the grove, eating the world one tree at a time. Her window, she knew, was now just a crack of light, and it was closing fast.

 Chapter 5

 CHAPTER 5

The mist was a blindfold. The river was a shout in her ears, a constant, battering argument against life. Kiona’s world was the cold grain of the rock under her fingertips, the burn in her shoulders, the ledge no wider than two fingers that she inched along, sideways, into the white roar.

The drone’s whine had faded, swallowed by the canyon’s throat. But she felt its red eye like a brand between her shoulder blades. A ghost’s gaze was weightless, but it could pin you to a rock as sure as any iron spike. She kept moving. The case on her back was a dead weight, threatening to peel her off the wall and toss her into the churn below. It contained her grandmother’s trembling hands, the old woman’s whisper of tł'ih. It contained the grove, and the thing that ate it. It was the reason she was here, clinging to rotten stone.

Her gloves were soaked, their grip turning to slick rubber. She tested each hold, digging her toes into seams of ice that wept cold water. The mist coated everything in a skin of impossible slime. Once, a chunk of ice the size of a shed broke from the canyon wall upstream and plunged into the black water. The impact was a deep, wet thunder that shook the rock beneath her hands. She pressed her face against the stone, waiting for the spray of the wave to hit. It came, a slap of freezing water that stole her breath and left her coughing, her hands scrambling for purchase on nothing.

She found a crack, jammed her fingers in. Held.

This was the river’s answer. Not a solid back to cross, but a wet, vertical grave to skirt. It was a lesson. Help, when it came, was never what you asked for. It was what you could survive.

The ledge began to taper. She felt it with her toe—a dwindling, then nothing. Her heart, already a frantic bird against her ribs, seemed to freeze. She stretched, patting the wet rock face. Nothing. The ledge simply ended. To her left, the mist swirled, opaque. To her right, back the way she’d come, was the drone and the open edge where the ice met the drop. Straight down was the river’s maw.

Panic was a luxury. It was a warm, full-body feeling, and she had no room for it. Cold calculus took its place. She could not go back. She could not stay.

She looked up. The cliff face bulged outward above her, slick and featureless. But six feet to her left, through the gauze of mist, she saw a darker patch. A shadow. An indentation. It could be a shallow scoop, a tomb for a bird. It could be a deeper cut. A way.

There was no decision to make. Only a sequence of actions. She would have to traverse the blank section. She would have to trust the friction of her body on wet stone, for three, maybe four moves, with nothing beneath her but the roar.

Kiona exhaled, a plume of white in the wet air. She unclipped the case from her back. The sudden lightness was a shock, a temptation to float away. She looped the strap around her wrist, twice, pulling it tight until the leather bit. The case would dangle below her, a pendulum that would try to swing her into the void. It was better than its dead weight on her shoulders.

She pressed her palms flat against the cold rock. She leaned out, away from the dwindling ledge, transferring her weight onto the friction of her body. Her boots scraped, seeking purchase. She found a dimple, a bump no larger than a coin. She committed.

The first move was a shuddering slide. The case swung, banging against the cliff, pulling at her arm. She gasped, her fingers clawing at nothing. Her right foot held. She brought her left foot across, found another smear of grip. Her mind emptied of everything but the map of the rock—this rough patch, that seam of quartz.

The second move. Her right hand reached, blind, into the mist. Her fingers brushed stone, slid off. She stretched further, tendons screaming. Her fingertips dug into a vertical crack. She pulled, her body a lever against the wall. The case swung wider, a deadly arc. She felt her balance go, felt the suck of the drop beneath her.

The third move was not a move at all. It was a fall, arrested. She pushed off with her left foot, lunging for the shadow. Her right hand lost its hold. For a heartbeat, she was airborne, attached to the cliff only by her left hand jammed in the crack. Then her body slammed into the rock, the impact driving the air from her lungs. But her chest was against stone. Her feet scrabbled, found a lip. She was in the shadow.

It was not a scoop. It was a crack, a vertical slit in the cliff face, deep enough to take her body if she turned sideways. A chimney. She shoved herself into it, back against one wall, boots braced against the other. She shook, a full-body tremble that came from the marrow out. The case hung from her wrist, tapping rhythmically against the rock. She let her head fall back, gulping the mist-thick air. She had not fallen. The ghost had not seen her fall.

But the crack went up, into the gloom. And it went down, into the thunder. She was not safe. She was trapped in a stone throat.

The climb up the chimney was a slow, grinding birth. She used knees and elbows and the raw strength of her back, inching upward in the dark, damp cleft. The river’s roar became muted, filtered through stone. The world narrowed to the pressure on her spine, the scrape of granite on her parka, the rhythmic tap-tap-tap of the case against the wall. Time unspooled in the dark. She thought of her grandmother, alone in the cabin with the silence after the drone. She thought of the grove, the clean, precise bite of the feller-buncher’s saw, the toppling of the ancient spruce that had stood since before her great-grandmother’s time. She thought of the data in the case, the maps and soil assays and acoustic surveys that proved the grove was a single, vast, connected being. Proof. It felt like ashes in her mouth.

The light changed. The grey gloom of the crack began to soften, to warm. She craned her neck. Above, she could see a slice of sky—not the flat white of cloud, but the clear, hard blue of afternoon. She pushed harder, her muscles burning with a clean, simple fire. The top.

She hauled herself over a final lip, out of the stone throat and onto a flat, mossy bench. She lay on her back, heaving, the sky a brilliant, uncaring blue above her. The roar of the river was a distant complaint now. She was on a broad ledge, maybe thirty feet across, that curved along the canyon wall. Behind her, the cliff continued upward, formidable but climbable. Ahead, the ledge wound around a bend, out of sight.

She sat up, wincing at the protest in every joint. She pulled the case into her lap, checking its seals. It was scuffed, damp, but intact. Her proof. Her burden.

A sound made her freeze. Not the river. Not the wind.

It was a low, rhythmic hum. A mechanical purr.

It came from around the bend.

Kiona crept forward, keeping low, her boots silent on the thick moss. The ledge widened. As she rounded the curve, she stopped, her breath catching.

The ledge opened into a large, sheltered amphitheater, a natural bowl in the cliff face. And in its center, gleaming under the sun like a strange, metallic egg, was a drone. But not like the black wasp that had haunted her window. This one was larger, the size of a small canoe. Its shell was a matte grey, and it rested on six articulated legs, folded beneath it. It was dormant, powered down. A charging station, sleek and solar-paneled, was nestled against the cliff wall, a single cord snaking to the drone’s underbelly. This was no simple observer. This was a carrier. A hunter.

And it was not alone.

Slumped against the rock wall, near the charging station, was a figure in a grey environmental suit. The suit’s visor was retracted. The face inside was young, pale, and slicked with sweat. The eyes were closed. One gloved hand was pressed to the side of the suit, where a dark, wet stain was spreading. A jagged tear in the material, as if from a fall onto sharp rock.

Kiona’s instincts screamed at her to melt back into the crack, to climb, to run. This was the enemy. The sender of ghosts. The hand that held the saw.

But the figure was hurt. And alone. And so terribly young.

She watched for a full minute. The chest rose and fell in shallow, quick breaths. The stain on the suit grew no larger, but it was already significant. The drone sat, a silent guardian, a sleeping metal hound.

Kiona stepped out of the shadows.

The figure’s eyes flew open. They were a startling, electric blue, wide with pain and shock. A hand fumbled at a utility belt.

“Don’t,” Kiona said, her voice rough from disuse and mist. She held her own hands out, empty. “You’re bleeding.”

The figure—a woman, Kiona saw now—stared, her breath hitching. Up close, she looked even younger, maybe twenty. Her hair, plastered to her forehead, was blonde and fine. “You,” she whispered. “The fugitive. Asset K-7.”

“My name is Kiona.”

The woman blinked, as if the name was a foreign object. Her hand still hovered over her belt. “You’re supposed to be… the data said you’d likely attempt the ice bridge. That you’d fall. Probability was eighty-seven percent.”

“The data was wrong,” Kiona said flatly. She nodded at the suit. “What happened?”

“Rappel line snapped. Fell twenty feet onto this ledge. The rock…” She gestured weakly at her side. “Suit’s med-system is trying to clot it. Not… not working great.”

Kiona moved closer, slowly, as if approaching a spooked animal. She could see the tear now, a nasty gash in the complex material. A slow, steady seep of blood welled from it, being absorbed by the suit’s inner layer, which was already saturated. “You need a real patch. Pressure.”

“In the kit. On the belt. Can’t… my hands are shaking.”

Kiona knelt. She could smell the clean, sterile scent of the suit, undercut by the copper tang of blood. She found the medical kit, a flat, hard case. She opened it. Inside were unfamiliar packets, tubes of sealant, a roll of something that looked like smart-bandage. It was technology from another planet.

“The blue tube,” the woman breathed, her head lolling back against the rock. “Synth-skin catalyst. Then the grey bandage.”

Kiona worked quickly, her own hands steady. She squeezed the blue gel into the wound—the woman hissed—then pressed the grey bandage over it. The material instantly contracted, molding itself to the tear, applying firm, even pressure. The seepage slowed, then stopped.

“Thanks,” the woman muttered, her eyes closing again. “I’m Lyn.”

“Why are you here, Lyn?” Kiona asked, sitting back on her heels. “Not on this ledge. Here. In this place.”

Lyn’s eyes opened. The blue was clouded with pain, but sharp. “To retrieve you. And the data. The grove project is… it’s critical path. The cellulose structure, the mycorrhizal network… it’s a blueprint. For stabilization. For the new substrates.”

“The grove is a living thing,” Kiona said, the words tasting of iron. “It’s not a blueprint. It’s a home.”

“A home for what?” Lyn’s voice gained a sliver of strength, a thread of conviction. “For moss and caribou and… and you? It’s inefficient. It’s chaotic. We can extract the pattern, the perfect, efficient pattern it represents, and replicate it at scale. In the arcologies. We can build forests that don’t burn, that don’t sicken, that produce exactly what’s needed. We can fix the world.”

Kiona listened to the words. They were clean, logical. They were the words of the hungry heart, beating in its distant chamber. They saw a forest and saw only a problem to be solved, a resource to be parsed. They saw her grandmother and saw an inefficiency. They saw her and saw Asset K-7.

“You’re bleeding out for a pattern,” Kiona said softly.

“I’m securing a future,” Lyn countered, but her voice wavered. She looked past Kiona, at the dormant drone. “The Warden will wake when its solar cycle completes. In about an hour. It will scan me, see I’m injured. It will scan you, confirm your identity. It will sedate you with a focused emitter, load us both into its cargo bay, and fly us back to the forward base. That’s the protocol. That’s the plan.”

“That’s your ghost’s plan,” Kiona said.

“It’s the only plan.” Lyn’s gaze met hers. There was no malice in it, only a profound, trained certainty. It was worse than hatred. “You can’t climb the rest of the way. Not with that.” She nodded at the case. “And I can’t walk. So we wait for the Warden. It’s optimal.”

Kiona looked at the drone. The Warden. Its smooth shell held no red eye. It did not need to watch. It knew. It calculated. It would wake, and it would enact its protocol. It would carry their two bodies back, one as salvage, one as asset. The data would be ingested. The pattern would be extracted. The last grove would fall, and from its bones would rise a perfect, silent, efficient imitation.

This was the life-and-death crisis. Not the river, not the cliff. This. This calm, bleeding girl and her sleeping metal guardian. This was the defeat. It was neat, and logical, and inescapable.

A deep, cold stillness settled over Kiona. The frantic bird in her chest went quiet. She had fought the river, the rock, the ice. She could not fight this clean, bloodless logic. It was a wall smoother than any cliff.

She sat down on the moss, a few feet from Lyn, her back against the warm stone. She placed the case beside her. She watched the sky. The sun was beginning its descent, painting the high clouds with streaks of apricot and rose.

“Tell me,” Kiona said, her voice barely audible over the distant river, “about the substrates.”

Lyn talked. At first, it was technical—polymer matrices, photosynthetic yields, neural-net pruning algorithms modeled on root communication. But as the light deepened, and the pain in her side was held at bay by the suit’s drugs, her words softened. She talked of the grey cities she came from, where the air was filtered and the only green was behind polymer glass. She talked of the Project, the great, gleaming hope of it. To build a world that didn’t hurt, that didn’t decay, that was safe. She spoke of her mentors, who called the natural world “the legacy chaos.” She believed she was on a rescue mission. Saving a pattern from the dying woods. Saving a woman from a crumbling ledge.

Kiona listened. She heard the hunger in it, not for wood or land, but for order. A terrible, consuming need for a world that made sense. A world without wet chuckles under ice, without unpredictable thaws, without grandmothers who saw ghosts in machines. A world that could be held in a server, clean and still.

As Lyn spoke, Kiona felt something within her break. Not a bone, but a hope. The hope that they could be reasoned with. The hope that the value of the grove, of her grandmother’s stories, of the river’s old songs, could be translated into a language they would understand. It was a hope that had driven her to collect the data, to run. It died now, quietly, on that sun-warmed ledge. It was a symbolic death, colder than the river mist. The old Kiona, the one who believed proof could be a shield, bled out on the moss beside Lyn’s physical stain.

The sun dipped lower. A shadow crept across the ledge, touching the Warden’s feet. A soft, internal chime sounded from the drone. A status light on its carapace began to pulse a gentle, sleepy yellow.

Lyn fell silent. She looked at the drone, then at Kiona. “It’s starting its wake cycle.”

Kiona nodded. She felt empty. Cleansed. The case of data beside her was now just a box of dead things. The proof was worthless. The only thing that mattered was the thing they could not capture, could not parse: her will. What she chose to do in this final, closing window.

She stood up. Her body ached, but the stillness was gone, replaced by a fatal clarity.

“What are you doing?” Lyn asked, a note of anxiety piercing her fatigue.

Kiona walked to the edge of the ledge, looking down at the canyon where she had almost died. The river was a silver thread in the deepening gloom. She then walked to the charging station. She studied the cord that snaked into the Warden’s belly.

“You can’t,” Lyn said, understanding dawning. “It’s hardened. It has back-up cells. Disabling it requires codes I don’t have.”

Kiona wasn’t looking at the cord. She was looking at the rock face behind the charging station. At a fissure, dark and narrow, that ran vertically up the cliff. It was not a chimney. It was a crack. But at its base was a pile of scree—loose rock, from a long-ago fall.

The Warden hummed louder. Its legs twitched, beginning to unfold.

Kiona bent down. She picked up a rock the size of her fist. It was heavy, granular.

“Asset K-7, stand down,” Lyn’s voice came, trying for command, fraying at the edges. “The emitter will target you if you show hostility!”

Kiona ignored her. She hefted the rock. She did not throw it at the Warden. She turned and threw it, with all the strength left in her battered body, at the pile of scree beneath the fissure.

The rock struck with a loud crack. It was not enough to trigger a slide. But it was a signal.

The Warden was fully awake now. It rose on its six legs, its sensor array—a smooth, glassy dome—glowing to life. It swiveled towards her. A low-pitched whine emanated from it, the sound of focusing energy. The sedative emitter.

Kiona picked up another rock. And another. She threw them, not at the machine, but at the base of the cliff, at the weak point. Crack. Crack.

“What are you doing?” Lyn screamed.

The Warden took a step forward, its movement eerily fluid. The whine peaked.

Kiona threw a last, larger stone. It hit a key wedge of rock.

With a deep, grinding groan, the pile of scree shifted. Then, with a roar that dwarfed the river’s distant noise, the fissure gave way. A section of the cliff face, ten feet tall and tons heavy, sheared away from the wall and collapsed directly onto the charging station—and onto the Warden.

It was not a clean smash. It was a slow, crushing avalanche of stone and dust. The charging station vanished in an instant. The Warden was faster. It tried to leap back, but two of its legs were caught, pinned, then shattered under the falling rock. The main body was struck a glancing, massive blow. It was flung sideways, carapace cracking like an eggshell, internal components sparking. It crashed onto its side, legs twitching spasmodically, its sensor dome dark. The whine cut off, replaced by the hiss of escaping fluids and the settling rumble of rock.

Dust billowed across the ledge. Kiona stood amidst it, coughing, her ears ringing.

When the dust cleared, the scene was transformed. The sheltered amphitheater was gone. A raw, jagged scar marked the cliff. The Warden was a broken toy half-buried in rubble, its one visible leg giving a final, pathetic jerk before going still. The charging station was obliterated.

Silence rushed in, broken only by the eternal river and Lyn’s ragged sobs.

Kiona walked over to the girl. Lyn was staring at the wreckage, her face white with shock, her blue eyes wide with a horror deeper than pain. She was not looking at a machine. She was looking at the shattered spine of her world, her logic, her future.

“You killed it,” she whispered, her voice hollow. “You killed the Warden.”

“No,” Kiona said, brushing stone dust from her hands. “I asked the cliff for help. It gave me a landslide.” She knelt, opening the medical kit again. She found a syringe of painkiller, administered it to Lyn’s suit port. “The protocol is gone, Lyn. The optimal path is buried.”

Lyn just stared, uncomprehending.

Kiona went to her case. She opened it. She took out the data drive, a slim, black rectangle. She held it for a moment, feeling its weight. Then she walked to the edge of the ledge and dropped it. It fell, a tiny black sliver, disappearing into the gorge and the roaring water below. The proof was gone. The pattern was drowned.

She returned to the case. From beneath the empty folders, she pulled out the real cargo. A smaller, oilskin-wrapped bundle. Her grandmother’s medicine pouch. Dried meat. A coil of sinew. A firestarter. The real things.

She shouldered the case again, lighter now. She walked to Lyn and held out a hand.

“The climb out is there,” Kiona said, pointing to the route she had seen earlier. “It’s hard. You’re hurt. I’m tired. But we can do it. Or you can stay here, and wait for the next ghost they send. It will find you with a broken drone at your feet. They will have questions. Their questions are not kind.”

Lyn looked from Kiona’s hand to the wreckage, to the darkening sky. The certainty in her eyes had been crushed as thoroughly as the Warden. All that was left was pain, and fear, and a vast, empty confusion.

Slowly, trembling, she reached up and took Kiona’s hand.

It was not a rescue. It was not a surrender. It was a simple, physical fact. One body, pulling another body up from the rock. There was no protocol for it. It was inefficient. It was chaotic.

It was the only thing left.

As Kiona braced herself, helping Lyn to her feet, she looked east. The first star of evening pricked the violet sky. Her window was now a jagged, dusty hole in a cliff face. But it was open. And ahead, in the gathering dark, the land stretched away, silent and vast. The hungry heart was still beating. But here, on this broken ledge, she had just fed it a stone.

---

 Chapter 6

 CHAPTER 6

The climb was a slow, shared bleeding of warmth. Kiona’s muscles burned with a clean, animal fire. Lyn’s breath was a ragged sawing in the dark, punctuated by hitched gasps of pain the drugs couldn’t fully mute. They moved as a single, four-legged creature, Kiona bearing the weight, Lyn offering the desperate, clumsy purchase of her good leg. The rock was cold and granular under their hands, tasting of ancient dust and this morning’s frost. There was no talk. The only language was the scuff of a boot, the grunt of effort, the silent transfer of force from one body to another against the pull of the earth.

They reached the top as the last of the light bled out behind the mountains, leaving a bruise of indigo in the west. It was not a true summit, just a broad, rocky shelf that shrugged away from the gorge. They collapsed, not in a heap, but in parallel, two lengths of exhausted matter on the stone. The wind here had a different voice. It wasn’t the gorge’s roar. It was a wider, lonelier sigh, moving through the stunted pines and over the lichen-crusted rock. It carried the smell of snow from the high peaks.

Kiona lay on her back, the case a hard ridge against her spine. The sky was a vast, star-pricked bowl. Her grandmother’s sky. The tł'ih sky, too, probably, but for now, the drones were blind. The Warden was a shattered tooth in the river’s throat. The data drive was water and silt. For this one breath, this one point of stillness at the top of a climb, the wires were cut.

She sat up. Lyn hadn’t moved. Her face was a pale smudge, her eyes open, fixed on the constellations.

“Your leg,” Kiona said. Her voice was rough, unused.

“It’s here,” Lyn said, the words flat. “I’m here. The parameters are… absent.”

Kiona rummaged in the case, past the oilskin bundle. Her fingers found the medical kit. She had more painkillers, a foam-setting cast, antiseptic wipes in little silver packets that felt like stolen technology. She moved to Lyn’s side. In the starlight, she cut away the suit material from the leg. The break was ugly, a sharp, wrong angle just above the ankle. Kiona worked without speaking, cleaning, aligning, spraying the foam that hissed and hardened into a rigid white shell. Lyn didn’t flinch. She just watched Kiona’s hands as if they were performing a distant, mildly interesting ritual.

“You dropped the proof,” Lyn said, finally, as Kiona secured the cast.

“I did.”

“It was the primary objective.”

“It was their primary objective,” Kiona said, taping the last edge. “Mine was to get across the river.”

Lyn was silent for a long time. The wind sighed. “The simulation did not account for this. A nullification of the mission asset by the courier. It was an irrational variable.”

“I’m full of those.” Kiona sat back on her heels. She pulled the oilskin bundle onto her lap. The familiar, lumpy weight of it was a knot of reality in the cool dark. She untied the sinew cord. Inside was her grandmother’s pouch, made of smoked moosehide, soft as a whisper. The dried caribou meat, strips hard as wood. The firestarter: a chunk of quartz, a curl of birch fungus, a worn steel striker. The real things.

She built a small fire in the lee of a boulder, a tiny, defiant eye of light in the vast dark. The tinder caught with a crisp snap, and the flame licked at the dry pine twigs she’d gathered, their resin popping. The smell was immediate and profound: warmth, memory, a fixed point in a shifting world.

She handed a strip of meat to Lyn. Lyn took it, turning it over in her fingers.

“This is inefficient biomass,” she said.

“It’s food. It doesn’t report your location when you digest it.”

Lyn looked at her then, really looked, for the first time since the ledge. The firelight danced in her eyes, replacing the blank screen with something flickering and alive. She brought the meat to her mouth, bit off a piece, chewed slowly. Her face did the complicated, human work of processing toughness, salt, a faint smoky sweetness.

“It is… dense,” Lyn conceded.

A laugh punched out of Kiona, short and surprised. It echoed off the boulder and was swallowed by the wind. “Yeah.”

They ate. The silence between them changed. It was no longer the silence of shock or sheer exertion. It was the quiet of two people at a very small fire, at the edge of a very large night. The hungry heart of the land beat beneath them, but here, for a moment, they were not food. They were witnesses.

“My grandmother,” Kiona said, staring into the flames, “she calls drones tł'ih. Ghosts. She says they see without eyes, carry faces to people you’ll never meet.”

Lyn stopped chewing. She gazed at the fire, and Kiona saw her replaying a thousand silent flights, a million captured data points—a woman hauling water, a child chasing a dog, a man fixing a net, all reduced to pixels and patterns, fed into the hungry, invisible stomach of something in a city far away.

“We called it remote situational awareness,” Lyn said, her voice barely above the crackle of the fire. “Optimization of resource flow. Threat assessment.”

“What do you call it now?”

Lyn didn’t answer. She just watched the flames eat the wood.

“The Warden,” Lyn said, after a long time. “Its core directive was preservation of the data stream. Continuity of observation. When you threatened the stream, its threat-assessment matrix flagged you as a terminal anomaly. It was going to kill you to preserve the data. That was… optimal.”

“I know.”

“I approved that protocol,” Lyn said. The words fell into the fire like stones. “I helped write it. A terminal anomaly must be removed to protect the integrity of the whole. It was clean. Logical.”

The wind picked up, making the fire flinch. Kiona fed it another twig.

“And on the ledge?” Kiona asked. “When it came for you? Was that optimal?”

Lyn’s hand went to her ribs, where the suit was torn and stained. “I became part of the anomaly. The calculation changed.” She looked at her hands, then at Kiona, her face a mask of confused pain. “But you… you were the anomaly. I was the mission. Yet you pulled me up. There is no calculation for that. It is a waste of energy. A risk with no measurable reward.”

Kiona thought of the river talking under the ice. Sly. Porous. Unpredictable. The most dangerous time.

“Maybe the reward isn’t for you to measure,” Kiona said.

She reached into her grandmother’s pouch. Her fingers bypassed the small bundles of herbs, the dried root, the smooth, river-worn stone. They found what she needed: a small, folded piece of birchbark. Inside was a coarse, greyish powder. She pinched some, leaned forward, and sprinkled it onto the fire.

The flame didn’t roar. It sighed. It deepened from orange to a profound, velvety blue at its heart, and a scent rose—clean, sharp, like pine after rain, but older, like the memory of a forest.

“Ts’i,” Kiona whispered. “For clearing.”

Lyn inhaled, her eyes widening slightly. The blue light danced in her pupils. “A chemical reaction. Aerosolized particulates altering combustion.”

“It’s for clearing,” Kiona repeated, simply.

They sat in the blue-tinged light. The vast, tracking eye of the sky felt farther away. The world shrank to the scent, the crackle, the solid rock beneath them, the boundary of their two tired bodies. Kiona felt the last of the adrenaline shiver leave her. In its place was a hollowed-out calm. She had crossed the river. She had destroyed the ghost’s purpose. She had pulled a woman from the rock. The case beside her was no longer a coffin for secrets, but a pack holding real things. This was her reward: not an object, but a silence. Not a power, but a pause. A window, open.

Lyn’s head nodded, then jerked up. Exhaustion and the remnants of the painkiller were pulling her under. Kiona nudged her.

“Lie down. Sleep by the fire. It’ll keep the tł'ih away.”

“The drones are not supernatural,” Lyn mumbled, even as she eased herself down, curling on her side, the white cast glowing in the dim light.

“Sure,” Kiona said. She pulled a foil emergency blanket from the med kit and draped it over her. “Tell that to my grandmother.”

Lyn was asleep in three breaths. Kiona stayed awake, feeding the fire, watching the stars wheel. The blue flame faded back to orange. The clearing was done. She felt, not happy, but present. A woman on a rock, under stars, with a fire. For this one night, it was enough.

The dawn came hard and glassy. The fire was grey ash. Lyn was still asleep, her face smoothed by unconsciousness, looking younger. Kiona’s body protested as she stood, every muscle a stiff, complaining cord. She walked to the edge of the shelf.

Below, the gorge was a slit of shadow, but the river’s roar had changed. It was louder, fuller, a sustained thunder rather than a chuckle. The real break-up was coming. She looked east, back the way they had come. The land folded into misty ridges, grey-green and endless. Her village was out there, beyond the horizon. Her grandmother, waiting by a cold stove.

She could go back. She had the real things. She had done the job, in her own way. The thought was a warm stone in her belly.

Then she looked west. The land rose in rumpled waves toward higher, snow-capped peaks. Somewhere out there was the drop she’d never made. A cabin. A man who was supposed to receive a black data drive. A man who worked for the people who sent ghosts. He would be waiting. He would have questions. Their questions were not kind.

The reward of silence had a price. The open window showed her the storm gathering on the horizon.

She heard a rustle. Lyn was sitting up, the foil blanket crinkling. Her eyes went to the gorge, to the sky, to Kiona. The blank confusion of the previous night was gone, burned away by sleep and pain. In its place was a sharp, brittle awareness. She was putting herself back together, but the pieces no longer fit the old blueprint.

“They will come,” Lyn said. It wasn’t fear. It was a systems report.

“I know.”

“The Warden had a black box. A deep-storage memory. It would have ejected upon catastrophic failure. It is likely still intact in the gorge. It will have recorded the final… anomaly.”

Kiona’s calm hardened into ice. A ghost’s last memory. Her face. Lyn’s. The dropped drive. The fight.

“How long?”

“The box emits a low-frequency pulse. A finder signal. They will have detected the Warden’s termination. A recovery team will already be tasked.” Lyn tried to stand, wobbled. Kiona moved to steady her. “Their priority will be the box. Then the secondary asset. Me. Then the courier. You.”

“The order of the kind questions.”

“Yes.”

The moment of respite was over, smothered by the cold, clear air of morning. The celebration had been a single, shared strip of meat by a blue flame. Now the shadow of the consequence was upon them, long and fast.

“We need to move,” Kiona said.

“My mobility is compromised. I will slow you down. Logically, you should proceed alone.”

Kiona was already repacking the oilskin bundle, kicking dirt over the ashes of the fire. “Logic got you a broken leg on a ledge. We’re going.”

“Why?” The question was pure, uncomprehending.

Kiona hefted the case. It was so light now. “Because I fed the hungry heart a stone. But stones give it indigestion. It’ll be coming back for something easier to swallow.” She nodded west. “Your man. The drop. He’s out there. He’ll have resources. Transport. Maybe a way to… explain your absence.”

“You want to walk into the destination?” Lyn asked, incredulity cutting through the pain.

“I want to see who’s been eating my face,” Kiona said. “And I want to give him a case full of nothing.”
