It was a mad plan. A chaotic, inefficient path. Lyn stared at her, and Kiona saw the old protocols flare and die in her eyes, finding no purchase. There was no simulation for this.

Slowly, Lyn nodded. It was not agreement. It was acknowledgment of a new, terrible variable.

They moved as the sun cleared the peaks, a hard, white light that showed no mercy. Kiona supported Lyn, their pace a torturous shuffle over the rocky terrain. Every sound—the screek of a jay, the rush of wind in a gully—was the whine of a drone. Every glint of sun on wet stone was the lens of a tł'ih.

They had been moving for two hours when Lyn froze, her weight going rigid against Kiona.

“Listen.”

Kiona heard it. Under the wind, under the distant river. A low, rhythmic thrum, felt in the bones more than heard. It was the sound of the world having a heartbeat, and the heartbeat was mechanical.

They crouched behind a spill of boulders. Kiona peered through a crack.

It came from the south, a sleek, grey dragonfly of composite and silent rotor. It was larger than a scout drone. A transport. It moved with purpose, following the line of the gorge, then slowing, beginning a slow, spiraling descent.

“The recovery team,” Lyn breathed. “They’ve found the signal.”

They watched as the aircraft hovered, then settled somewhere out of sight, down in the ragged lip of the gorge. The thrumming idle of its engines was a corrupt vibration in the ancient air.

The shadow wasn’t just coming. It had landed.

Kiona’s hand went to her grandmother’s pouch, felt the solid shape of the river stone inside. A real thing. She looked from the hidden aircraft to the vast, trackless wilderness to the west. The open window now had a sentry posted outside.

The man at the drop would be waiting. The ghosts were picking through the wreckage. And she was here, in the middle, with a broken asset and a case full of sinew and stories.

Lyn’s voice was a dry whisper. “What is your protocol now?”

Kiona watched a tendril of dust rise from the gorge where the aircraft had settled. The hungry heart had indigestion, and it had sent its teeth to investigate.

She met Lyn’s eyes. “We run.”

---

 Chapter 7

 CHAPTER 7

The run was not a run. It was a stumble, a lurch, a desperate consumption of ground that tasted of iron and old snow. The mechanical heartbeat from the gorge was a bass note to their flight, a vibration that climbed the rock and up through the soles of Kiona’s boots. It said found, found, found.

Lyn was a ghost of efficiency, pain pared down to a sharp, focused light in her eyes. She said nothing, saving every atom of breath for motion. Kiona matched her, an arm around her waist, their two bodies moving as one clumsy, determined beast. They did not head west, toward the open wilderness. That was a dream now, a painting behind glass. The dragonfly had settled between them and that dream. Instead, Kiona angled them north, following the high shoulder of land above the gorge, keeping to the skeletal lee of granite spines and the brittle cover of last year’s fireweed.

The river was louder here. The thaw-song had become a wet, grinding argument. It covered the sound of their boots on scree, but it also covered the sound of anything that might be coming for them. The world was all mouth, talking over itself.

They had gone half a mile when the first shout echoed up from the gorge. A human sound, blunt and technological in its clarity. It was followed by another, and a burst of static-garbled speech. The teeth were talking to each other.

Kiona pulled Lyn down behind a fallen spruce, its roots a gnarled wall. They lay in the damp rot, breathing the smell of fungus and cold earth. Below, through a lattice of branches, they saw movement. Figures in grey environmental suits, moving with the unburdened purpose of wasps around a nest. They were clustered at a point on the opposite wall of the gorge. The wreckage. One figure pointed upward, tracing a line from the wreckage to the rim where Kiona and Lyn had been that morning. The gesture was a cold vector. It drew a line directly through their previous position.

“They have the trajectory,” Lyn whispered, her lips barely moving. “They’ll extrapolate a search cone.”

Kiona didn’t ask how she knew. The knowledge was in the flatness of her tone. This was procedure. The ghost finds the body, the teeth measure the wound, they calculate the spray. They were inside the cone now. Its point was widening, swallowing the land.

“We need to get across the river,” Kiona said.

Lyn’s head turned, her eyes sharp. “The ice is singing.”

“It’s still holding. Just. And they’ll expect us to avoid it. They’ll look for us trying to outrun them on land. The river is a door they think is locked.”

It was the kind of thinking her grandmother would call ǫǫ̀, the cunning of the hare that doubles back under its own scent. The thought of the old woman, alone in the cabin with the memory of the dawn drone, was a physical ache in Kiona’s chest. She pushed it down. That ache was a luxury for the still, for the safe.

They moved again, a slower, more agonizing traverse now, cutting downward toward the river’s roar. The land steepened, funneling them into a chute of broken shale that clattered and shifted like a pile of dry bones. Every sliding step was a announcement. They froze each time, becoming stone, but the river’s voice was a greedy ally, swallowing the smaller sounds.

They reached the bank where the ice was not a smooth sheet, but a chaos of upheaval. The thaw had swollen the water beneath, and the ice had buckled, creating a jagged landscape of blue-white plates and pressure ridges. It groaned, a deep-throated complaint from the planet’s joints. In the center, a black seam of open water churned, vomiting chunks of milky ice.

“There,” Kiona said, pointing upstream to where a massive sweep of driftwood—whole trees, stripped and pale—was frozen into the ice margin, creating a tangled blind. “We cross from behind that. The ice will be thicker near the shore debris. We go one at a time. You first.”

Lyn didn’t argue. She was a creature of thresholds now, of calculated risk. She nodded, her face pale as the ice. Kiona helped her down the final bank, through the brittle willows, and into the frozen cage of the driftwood. The world reduced to the roar, the groan, and the smell of wet stone and splintered pine.

Lyn stepped onto the ice. It was not a step, but a transfer of weight, an offering. The ice accepted it with a muffled grunt. She took another, arms wide for balance, her body a tense line of concentration. Kiona watched, her own breath held, as Lyn picked a path between the ridges, moving with the fragile grace of a insect on a pond skin. She was halfway across the seventy-yard span when the drone rose from the gorge.

It was the scout, the black wasp. It didn’t emerge from below them, but from downstream, having circled wide. It rose on a column of silent air until it hovered, a stark period against the white sky, a hundred yards away. It hung there, observing. The eye without a face.

Lyn saw it. She froze. A statue on the groaning plate.

Kiona’s hand was in her grandmother’s pouch, fingers closing around the river stone. A real thing. She didn’t throw it. She held it, its weight an anchor. See this, she thought at the drone. See a girl holding a rock. See nothing.

The drone pivoted, slightly. Its lens was a dark pupil. It was looking at Lyn.

Then it began to move. Not toward Lyn, but in a slow, sweeping arc toward Kiona’s position on the bank. It was methodical, painting the area with its sight. It would see her in the driftwood in three seconds.

Kiona did the only thing she could. She broke from cover.

She ran not onto the ice, but along the bank, away from Lyn, crashing through the willows with no attempt at stealth. She was a loud, clumsy animal, a distraction. The drone’s hum pitched higher. It changed course, locking onto her, accelerating.

“Kiona!” Lyn’s shout was torn away by the river.

Kiona ran. The bank was soft and treacherous, pocked with snow and frozen mud. She slipped, scrambled up, kept going. The drone was faster. It closed the distance, its shadow a jumping spider over the ground ahead of her. It wasn’t just observing now. It was herding. It drove her toward a bare, open stretch of bank where the trees fell away.

A second sound joined the river and the drone’s whine. The thrum-thrum-thrum of the transport’s engines, powering up from the gorge. The teeth were mobilizing.

Kiona skidded to a halt. The open bank was a trap. The drone hovered twenty feet away, a sentinel. Behind her, the way was blocked by a steep, muddy cut-bank. She was cornered.

The drone edged closer. From its underbelly, a slender manipulator arm unfolded, delicate and precise. At its tip, a hypodermic needle gleamed. Not just an eye. A stinger.

Protocol. Secure the asset. Neutralize the interference.

Kiona’s back was to the cut-bank. She had the river stone in one hand. The case with its sinew and stories was a heavy guilt on her back. The drone dipped, preparing its dart.

She didn’t throw the stone at the drone. She threw it at the cut-bank beside her, aiming for a thick seam of exposed, waterlogged soil just above her head. The stone struck with a wet thud. It did nothing.

The drone descended another foot, the needle orienting.

Kiona scrabbled at the bank, her fingers digging into the frozen mud, tearing at the seam. She pulled a great, dripping clod of earth free and hurled it, not at the machine, but into the air between them. It exploded in a shower of dirt.

The drone hesitated, its sensors momentarily confused by the particulate cloud.

Kiona dug again, heaving another mass of soil and root. She was not fighting the machine. She was fighting the bank, mining it, making a mess of the air. The drone weaved back, its precise programming offended by the chaos.

Then, with a sound like a giant’s stomach growling, the section of bank she was undermining gave way.

It wasn’t a collapse. It was a slump. A ton of saturated earth and rock peeled away from the riverside, carrying Kiona with it in a churning, roaring avalanche of mud. She was tumbling, drowning in cold earth, the world upended. She hit the river ice not as a person, but as a geological event.

The ice screamed.

A web of fractures lightninged out from the point of impact. The great plate shuddered. On the far bank, Lyn cried out.

Kiona was half-buried in the slide, spitting mud, her legs pinned by debris. The ice beneath her groaned, a sound of profound structural regret. The black seam of open water downstream widened, hungry.

The drone, having pulled up to avoid the slide, now repositioned. It dropped toward her again, stinger extended. It would dose her where she lay, let the cold and the mud finish the work.

A smaller rock struck its central rotor housing.

It was a perfect throw. It hit with a sharp tock. The drone wobbled, its hum turning ragged. It spun, seeking the source.

Lyn stood on the far bank, arm still extended. She had another rock in her hand. Her face was a mask of pure, furious defiance. She threw the second rock. It clipped a rotor blade. The drone’s flight became a drunken stagger.

It was the opening. Kiona heaved, ignoring the pain in her legs, wrenching herself free of the mud. She was on her hands and knees on the fractured ice. The cracks sang a high, terrible tune. She crawled, then staggered upright, aiming for the driftwood blind on the opposite shore, for Lyn.

The drone, recovering, ignored Lyn and shot after Kiona. But its flight was damaged, its trajectory a wobbling line. It overshot, corrected, and in that moment of mechanical confusion, it passed directly over the widening black seam in the ice.

From the open water, a long, gnarled branch—a snag from the bottom—rolled up in the current. It rose like a skeletal arm and caught in the drone’s landing struts.

The machine jerked. It tried to ascend, but the water-logged timber was a stubborn anchor. The rotors screamed, biting air, then water. For three seconds, it was a terrible, struggling creature. Then the cold river gulped it down. The black wasp was gone, leaving only a swirl of bubbles in the black water.

The silence it left was immense, filled only by the river’s roar.

Kiona reached the far bank. Lyn grabbed her arm, hauling her the last steps onto solid ground. They collapsed together in the snow, gasping, coated in mud and fear.

“The stone,” Kiona panted, patting her grandmother’s pouch. It was empty. “I lost it.”

Lyn just shook her head, her eyes on the gorge. The thrum of the transport was louder, angrier. A different sound now—the whine of terrestrial engines, of tracked vehicles. The teeth were coming on the ground.

“They’ll have seen that,” Lyn said. “They’ll come faster.”

Kiona pushed herself up. Her body was a ledger of complaints. “Then we stop running.”

Lyn stared at her. “Your protocol is to stand and fight?”

“No,” Kiona said, looking north, toward the distant, unseen ribbon of the winter road. “My protocol is to deliver the package. The man at the drop is still waiting. He’s our only exit. But the teeth are between us and him now. We can’t outrun their machines. So we make the machines irrelevant.”

“How?”

“We go where they can’t follow. We go into the old places.”

Lyn’s expression shifted. The asset recognized the parameters. “Non-technical terrain. Navigation hazards.”

“The Caves of the Children,” Kiona said. It was not a place you said lightly. It was a story-place, a ǫǫ̀ place, where the land ate sound and light and spat out confusion. “They run like veins under this ridge. They come out near the old landing strip. Near the drop.”

“You know these caves?”

“I know stories.” Kiona adjusted the case on her back. It felt heavier. “My grandfather got lost in them for three days. He said the stones in there… remember. He said they drink your light and give back echoes of things that haven’t happened yet.”

It was the kind of thing her grandmother would say. Lyn processed it not as poetry, but as data. “Acoustic anomalies. Magnetic interference. Sensor degradation.”

“Yes,” Kiona said. “All of that.”

The first vehicle, a low-slung tracked crawler with a composite hull, nosed over the rim of the gorge a quarter-mile downstream. A searchlight, pale in the daylight, swept the bank.

Kiona took Lyn’s hand. It was cold and firm. “We have to go into the dark. Can you do that?”

Lyn looked from the approaching light to Kiona’s mud-streaked face. She saw no protocol there. Only a terrible, specific will. She nodded.

They turned their backs on the river, on the light, and entered the tree line. The forest here was thick, a cathedral of spruce and shadow. They did not go far before Kiona found it: a cleft in a moss-covered rock face, half-hidden by a fallen hemlock. It was not a cave mouth, but a sigh. A slit in the world’s skin, exhaling air that was several degrees colder and smelled of damp stone and time.

Kiona pulled the hemlock boughs aside. The darkness within was absolute.

“Stay close,” she said, her voice already swallowed by the rock. “Don’t let go.”

She stepped in. Lyn, after the briefest hesitation, followed.

The light from outside lasted for three steps. Then it was gone, as if a door had been shut. The roar of the river vanished, replaced by a dense, ringing silence, punctuated by the drip of water somewhere in an unimaginable deep. The air was a cold blanket. Kiona flicked on her small headlamp. The beam was a pathetic thing, a child’s candle against the enormity of the dark. It showed a tunnel of smooth, water-sculpted limestone, narrowing ahead.

They moved in a bubble of frail light, their breath pluming in the beam. The cave did not consent to their passage. It tolerated it. The floor was uneven, slick with calcite. The walls pressed close, then opened into chambers so vast Kiona’s light could not find the ceiling, only catching on distant, gleaming stalactites like stone teeth in a giant’s maw. In these chambers, the silence had a texture. It was the sound of listening.

Lyn moved like a ghost beside her, her training making her footfalls silent. But her breathing was loud here. The cave made it loud.

“This is insane,” Lyn whispered, and the cave took the word, insane, and passed it back to them from three different directions, a soft, sighing argument.

“Don’t speak,” Kiona murmured. “The stones here… they like to talk.”

They went deeper. Kiona navigated by memory of stories, by the feel of the air moving (always go toward the moving air, her grandfather had said), by the ancient, invisible paths worn by water and time. She tried not to think of the weight of the mountain above them. She tried not to think of the case on her back, whose contents now felt less like data and more like a stolen piece of someone’s soul, brought into a place that knew all about souls.

After an hour, Lyn stopped. “Do you hear that?”

Kiona listened. Drip. Silence. The hum of her own blood. Then, faintly, a new sound. A vibration, coming through the rock. A distant, rhythmic thud-thud-thud. It was the transport’s engine, but muffled, translated by stone. It was above them. They were moving parallel to the search.

“They’re on the ridge,” Kiona said. “They know we didn’t cross the river further down. They’re guessing we’re in the trees.”

“They will have thermal,” Lyn said. “They will scan the mountain.”

“The rock is thick here. And cold. We’re a whisper in a freezer.”

They pressed on. The tunnel forked. Kiona chose the left-hand path, where the air smelled less stagnant. This path descended, coiling down in a tight spiral. The thud-thud-thud from above faded, replaced by a new sound: a low, subterranean wind, a sigh from the earth’s lungs. The air grew colder, sharper.

The tunnel opened into a chamber that made Kiona’s breath catch. Her headlamp beam skittered across a lake of absolute blackness, water so still it was a void. The chamber was vast, the far wall lost in darkness. Stalactites and stalagmites met in the center, forming a forest of stone columns. And the ceiling… the ceiling was covered in a glittering, crystalline frost. Not ice. Gypsum flowers, blooming in eternal winter.

It was breathtaking. It was a trap.

The path around the lake was a narrow ledge, slick with moisture. To the left, the black water. To the right, a sheer rock wall.

“Single file,” Kiona said. “Go slow. Test every step.”

Lyn went first, her hands pressed to the wall for balance. Kiona followed, her beam lighting Lyn’s careful feet. The ledge was uneven, sometimes crumbling. Pebbles dislodged by their boots plinked into the water below. The sound was deep, a lonely plonk that echoed for a long time.

They were halfway around when Kiona’s light caught something on the lake’s surface. A ripple. Then another. Not from their falling pebbles. From the center of the lake.

Something broke the water.

It was pale, long. A shape. It wasn’t an animal. It was a tendril of mist, coiling up from the blackness, glowing with a faint, internal bioluminescence. Then another. And another. Dozens of them, rising like ghostly fingers, waving in the still air. They pulsed with a soft, blue light.

Lyn froze. “What is that?”

Kiona’s grandfather’s story echoed in her head. “The Children,” he’d said, his voice hushed. “Not real children. The old things that live in the deep water. They look like lost souls. They are cold, and they are lonely. And they want you to join them.”

“Don’t look at them,” Kiona said, her voice tight. “Keep moving. Look at the wall. At your feet.”

But the light was mesmerizing. The tendrils—whether mineral, fungal, or something else—swayed, and as they swayed, they began to emit a sound. A faint, crystalline hum, just at the edge of hearing. It was a song without notes. It vibrated in Kiona’s teeth.

Lyn took a step, her foot slipping on the wet rock. She cried out, arms windmilling. Kiona lunged, grabbing the strap of Lyn’s pack, hauling her back from the edge. But the momentum was too much. They both stumbled. The case on Kiona’s back slammed against the rock wall.

The latch, stressed by the fall, the mud, the cold, gave way.

The case sprang open.

Kiona felt it happen—the sudden shift of weight, the click-pop. She twisted, but it was too late. The contents spilled out into the dark.

The data cores, the crystal drives, the sealed biostorage modules—they tumbled from the open case, bouncing on the narrow ledge. Some skittered toward the wall. Three, four, five of them arced over the edge and fell toward the black lake.

Time slowed. Kiona watched the most valuable, dangerous objects in her life—the reason for all of this—pinwheel in her headlamp beam. They struck the surface of the water not with a splash, but with a series of soft, final glugs.

They were gone.

The glowing tendrils near the impacts recoiled, then swarmed toward the sinking objects, coiling around them, their blue light intensifying, as if feeding on the strange, technological morsels.

Kiona stood, paralyzed, the empty case a hollow shell on her back. The mission, the deal, her way out—it was sinking into a primeval lake, wrapped in glowing tendrils. A scream built in her throat, a silent, useless thing.

Lyn was staring at the water, her face blank with shock. Then her eyes snapped to Kiona. Not with accusation. With a terrible, clear understanding.

The remaining items lay scattered on the ledge. A few data cores. The biostorage module that held the physical samples—the sinew. Maybe half the payload.

Kiona dropped to her knees, scrambling for them, her hands numb. She shoved them back into the broken case, forcing the latch closed. It wouldn’t hold. She tore a strap from her own pack, binding it shut.

When she looked up, Lyn was still staring at the water. The ghost-light played on her face.

“The mission…” Lyn began.

“Is compromised,” Kiona finished, the words ashes in her mouth. “I know.”

“Without the full dataset, the analysis is incomplete. The value is… fractional.”

Kiona stood, the patched case in her hands. It felt like a corpse. “What is your protocol now?” she asked, throwing Lyn’s old question back at her.

Lyn was silent for a long moment. The cave hummed around them. Finally, she spoke, her voice detached, analytical. “Asset recovery of a compromised package is low priority. Containment of loose ends becomes primary.”

Kiona understood. She was a loose end. Lyn, by association, was a loose end. The man at the drop would not be happy. He might not be paying. He might be cleaning.

“We can’t go to the drop,” Kiona said.

“No,” Lyn agreed. “We cannot.”

“Then we go out the other side. We disappear.”

“They will not stop looking. For the asset. For the data. For us.” Lyn’s eyes met hers in the gloom. “There is a protocol for this, too. For agents in the wind. They call it ‘gardening.’ You prune the problem. You burn the cuttings.”

The cold of the cave seeped into Kiona’s bones. “You’re saying they’ll kill us.”

“I am saying my value was the data in my head and the data in that case,” Lyn said, nodding to the bound shell. “One is damaged. The other is… attached to you. A liability. They will not spend resources to retrieve a liability. They will spend resources to erase it.”

The truth was a cold, clear lake of its own. They were not running to anything anymore. They were just running.

The crystalline hum from the lake intensified. The glowing tendrils had dragged the lost drives deep, their light now just a faint smear in the abyss. The cave had eaten its share.

“Come on,” Kiona said, her voice hollow. “We need to keep moving. The air is moving. There’s a way out.”

They continued, the joy of the mission extinguished, replaced by a grim, survivalist dread. The cave seemed to sense their despair. It tightened around them. The tunnels grew smaller, requiring them to crawl on hands and knees through icy runoff. Their clothes were soaked, their bodies shaking with cold and adrenaline crash.

Kiona’s headlamp began to flicker. The battery was dying. In the failing light, the shadows grew teeth.

After an age of crawling, the air changed. It smelled less of stone and more of pine and snow. A faint grey light seeped from ahead, not the white of day, but the gloom of twilight.

They emerged not with a dramatic exit, but with a crawl into a thicket of dwarf willow under a lowering sky. They were on the north side of the ridge. The land fell away in a series of frozen benches toward the wide, flat valley of the winter road. In the distance, Kiona could see the lonely, weathered shack of the old landing strip. The drop point.

It was maybe three miles away across open, snowy ground.

And between them and it, parked on the ridge a mere five hundred yards from their cave exit, was the grey transport dragonfly. Its engines were off. It sat silent, a sleeping metal insect. Near it, two figures in grey suits stood by a portable comms array, their backs turned.

They had outflanked the search. They had come out behind the sentry.

But the sentry was still there.

Kiona killed her headlamp. They lay in the willow thicket, watching. The wind had picked up, blowing snow in granular streams across the ground. It would cover their tracks. It would also hide any sound of their approach.

“We go around,” Kiona whispered. “Keep to the tree line. Work our way down to the road, then follow it to the strip from the east.”

Lyn shook her head. She was looking not at the transport, but at the landing strip shack. “He will be there. Waiting. Or his people will be. You no longer have what he paid for. You have a problem. I am a problem.”

“So we don’t go to him. We bypass the strip. We follow the road west. We walk out.”

“To where?” Lyn’s question was not despairing. It was a genuine request for data. “Your grandmother’s cabin is compromised. There is no ‘out’ in this wilderness that their satellites and drones cannot eventually find. We are tired, injured, and carrying a beacon of failure.” She nodded at the case.

Kiona knew she was right. The open window was gone. The world had shrunk to this frozen hillside, with enemies in front and behind, and a stolen future sinking in a cave lake.

One of the figures by the transport turned. He lifted a pair of field glasses, scanning the tree line. His gaze swept past their thicket, paused, swept back.

He had seen something. A movement. A glint.

He said something to his partner, pointing. Both figures turned. They began walking, purposefully, toward the thicket.

They were out of time.

“Run,” Kiona said.

But Lyn didn’t run. She reached over and placed a cold hand on Kiona’s arm. Her touch was startlingly gentle. “You have a choice. A final variable.”

“What choice?”

“Give me the case.”

Kiona stared at her. “What?”

“Their primary target is the asset and the data. I am the asset. The remaining data is there.” She looked at the bound case. “If I present myself, with the package, it will consume their attention. It is the protocol. Recover the priority. They will focus on secure extraction. You will be a secondary concern. A witness in the wilderness. You might slip through the cracks in the confusion.”

Kiona’s mind reeled. “They’ll kill you. You said it yourself. ‘Gardening.’”

“Perhaps. Or perhaps my value, even diminished, warrants repatriation and… debriefing.” The word was a euphemism that hung in the cold air, ugly and full of pain. “It is a calculated risk. Better odds than both of us dying out here in the snow tonight.”

The two grey suits were closer now, fifty yards away, their movements cautious but deliberate. They had unslung compact rifles from their shoulders.

“You’re betraying me,” Kiona said, the hurt raw and sudden.

“No,” Lyn said, and for the first time, her voice held a flicker of something that wasn’t analysis. It was sorrow. “I am using my function to create an exit. For you. It is the only piece of the mission that is not compromised. Your survival.”

“I won’t let you.”

“It is not your decision to make.” Lyn’s hand moved from Kiona’s arm to the strap of the case. Her fingers were deft. With a quick twist, she had it unclipped from Kiona’s grasp.

“Lyn, don’t!”

Lyn stood up.

She rose from the willow thicket, a pale, battered figure against the grey sky, the broken case held in both hands before her like an offering. Or a shield.

The two grey suits stopped. Their rifles came up, trained on her.

Lyn took a step forward, then another, walking steadily toward them, away from Kiona’s hiding place.

“Asset designation: Lyra,” she called out, her voice clear and empty on the wind. “Requesting secure extraction. Package is… degraded. But present.”

One of the suits spoke into his collar mic. The other kept his rifle aimed at Lyn’s center mass.

Kiona watched, buried in the frozen thicket, her heart a stone. She saw Lyn walk into the open, saw the suits close around her, one taking the case, the other gripping her arm. She saw Lyn look back, once, toward the thicket. Her face was calm. Resolved. Then she was turned, marched toward the waiting transport.

The dragonfly’s engines whined to life. The rotors began to turn, slowly at first, then blurring into grey discs. The wind from them blasted the snow into a stinging cloud.

Kiona pressed her face into the frozen earth. She heard the pitch of the engines rise, felt the vibration through the ground. When she looked up, the transport was lifting off, turning south, carrying its recovered asset and its degraded prize back toward the world of teeth and hungry hearts.

She was alone.

The sacrifice hung in the air, colder than the wind. Lyn had traded herself for a crack in the door. A variable.

Kiona waited until the sound of the engines had faded completely, eaten by the vast, indifferent land. Then, moving like an old woman, she pushed herself up. She had no case. No partner. No plan.

She had only the direction Lyn had given her: away.

She turned north, away from the landing strip, away from the road, away from everything. She walked into the gathering dusk, into the endless white. The ghosts had taken their payment. The river’s voice was a distant memory. The only sound was the crunch of her own boots in the snow, a tiny, persistent protest against the silence.

And the knowledge, a cold seed in her gut, that they were not done with her. A witness was still a loose end. And the wilderness, for all its size, was not big enough to hide from a heart that had learned to beat in a machine. It would send more tł'ih. It would send teeth.

But for now, there was only the next step. And the next.

Behind her, in the cave of lost children, in the deep, black water, the ghost-light pulsed around the sunken drives. And in the silence of stone, something began to remember. Something began to dream.

---

 Chapter 8

 CHAPTER 8

The silence was a third presence, walking beside her. It had been two days, or maybe three—the flat, grey light and the endless white had dissolved time into a single, stretching moment. Kiona moved through it like a needle through felt, leaving a trail of stitches the wind would soon pick out. Her grandmother’s words were a dry rattle in her skull: When the land is this quiet, it is holding its breath. Something is coming to fill the space.

The something was hunger, cold, and the raw, animal knowledge that she was being followed.

It wasn’t a sound. It was a subtraction. The absence of a bird call where one should have been. The way the snow-laden spruce branches stopped their creaking a moment too soon. A tł'ih did not need to make noise. It simply made the world around it quieter, sucking the life out of the air before it arrived. They were patient. They had the patience of a starved thing that knows its meal cannot run forever.

Her own patience was a frayed cord. Lyn’s sacrifice was a weight in her chest, a lump of cold iron that made every breath an effort. She had traded herself for a variable. For a crack. Kiona carried the crack with her, a splinter of impossible hope lodged in her heart, and it hurt more than the cold.

On the third day, the land began to change. The flat white tundra buckled and fell away into a jagged scar of a canyon, a deep, shadowed gash as if the earth had been clawed open. At its bottom, she could hear the faint, frantic chatter of open water—a river that had shrugged off its ice too early. The far side was a sheer wall of striated rock, impossible. Her way was blocked.

She stood at the crumbling edge, looking down. This was the end of ‘away’. The wilderness, for all its size, had funneled her here, to this precipice. It was a perfect place for an ending.

The hum arrived not as a sound, but as a vibration in her teeth. It was the taste of ozone and burnt plastic. She didn’t turn. She kept her eyes on the river below, a thin, black snake writhing in its bed of stone.

“Kiona Mose.”

The voice was smooth, genderless, filtered through a speaker. It came from behind and above. The hover-drone. Not the small, wasp-like scout, but a larger model, a mantis with long, jointed arms folded against its carbon-fibre belly. It hung in the air ten meters behind her, a black cross against the grey sky.

“You are a significant logistical anomaly,” the voice stated. The words were clean, surgical. “You will be retrieved.”

This was the teeth the machine-heart was sending. Not a squad of suits, but a single, efficient tool. A scalpel for a loose end.

Kiona slowly turned. The drone’s lens, a dark, multifaceted orb, regarded her without curiosity. It saw biomass. A problem to be corrected. She saw her own reflection, distorted and small, in its polished shell.

“My partner,” Kiona said, her voice rough from disuse. “Lyn. Where is she?”

“Asset Lyn Chen has been reconciled. Her value has been reabsorbed.” The drone tilted a degree. “Your value is negative. You are a debt.”

The words were meant to be final. A closing of the ledger. But in them, Kiona heard the lie. If she were just a debt, they would have fired a tranquilizer dart, a net, a bullet. They were talking. The machine was assessing. Lyn’s variable was at work. The crack was letting in a draft.

The drone unfolded one of its arms. A needle-tipped probe gleamed. “You will submit to a final audit. Neural compliance will streamline the retrieval.”

A final, purified test. Not of her strength, but of her self. They wanted to look inside, to see what the wilderness and the sacrifice had written there. To see if the crack had grown. And then they would seal it, with her silence.

Kiona took a step back. Her heel sent a pebble skittering over the edge into the void. There was nowhere to go but down.

“Submission is the optimal pathway,” the voice intoned. The drone drifted forward, the probe extending.

This was the moment. The corner. The purified test. She had no case, no partner, no plan. She had only what the land had given her: the memory of the river’s wet chuckle, the weight of her grandmother’s fear, the cold resolve in Lyn’s eyes as they took her. And the thing growing in the silence, the thing beginning to dream in the deep, black water.

She did not look at the probe. She looked at the lens. She let the cold seed in her gut unfurl.

“You send your tł'ih to watch,” she said, her voice low, merging with the wind. “But you don’t know what the tł'ih see. You only know what they report. You see without eyes. You have no heart to beat. You are a ghost asking a ghost for permission to exist.”

The drone paused. Its hover-stabilizers whined, adjusting. “Your semantic noise is non-productive.”

“My grandmother called you tł'ih,” Kiona continued, taking another step back. The edge was close. The river’s chatter was louder, hungry. “But a ghost is just a story that won’t stay buried. A memory that haunts. You took Lyn. You are a memory of that theft. And I,” she said, and now she smiled, a thin, cold crack in her own face, “I am the thing that remembers.”

She saw it then, in the data-stream of her own desperation. The ultimate evil was not the machine, nor the men who sent it. It was the silence it demanded. The reconciliation. The reabsorption. It was the end of stories. Lyn had not traded herself for a weapon or a code. She had traded herself for a story—the story of her own defiance, now a ghost in the system, a variable that could not be reconciled.

The drone’s arm shot forward, fast as a serpent’s strike.

Kiona didn’t try to dodge. Instead, she dropped. Not backward over the cliff, but straight down, collapsing onto the frozen ground. The probe hissed through the air where her neck had been. As she fell, her hand scrabbled in the snow, finding not a weapon, but a stone. A fist-sized, jagged piece of the ancient riverbed.

The drone corrected, dipping lower, its arm retracting for another strike. It was now directly over her, between her and the safe ground.

Kiona rolled onto her back, brought the stone up, and did not throw it at the drone’s body or its lens. She threw it, with all the strength of her despair and her remembering, at the canyon wall beside them.

The stone struck the exposed face of ice and rock with a sharp, insignificant crack.

For a second, nothing. The drone’s probe re-oriented, pointing at her heart.

Then the land answered.

The cliff face was not solid. It was a sheet of wind-slab over rotten ice, a giant, precarious tooth. The impact was the final tap. With a groan that started deep in the earth, a fracture line shot upward, zigzagging like white lightning. Then the whole face sheared away.

It fell not as a single piece, but as a roaring avalanche of ice, rock, and snow. A white tsunami.

The drone’s logic would have been perfect for a thousand scenarios. Evasion, pursuit, target acquisition. It was not prepared for the entire world to collapse beside it. It tried to rise, to dart away, but the violent downdraft of the falling mass caught it, slamming it downward. A boulder the size of a ground-car clipped one of its rotor arms. The carbon fibre splintered with a sound like a giant bone breaking.

Kiona was already moving, scrambling crab-like on her hands and heels, pushing back from the edge as the world disintegrated. Snow and debris blasted over her, a choking, blinding cloud. She buried her face in the crook of her arm, felt the ground shudder as thousands of tons of the canyon’s lip met the river far below.

The roar seemed to last forever. When it faded, replaced by a settling hiss of snow-dust, the silence returned. But it was a different silence. It was the silence after a great breath has been expelled.

Kiona pushed the snow off her back and raised her head.

The geography had been rewritten. The canyon’s edge was now a raw, scooped-out bowl. And ten feet from the new precipice, half-buried in rubble and snow, lay the tł'ih.

It was broken. One arm was gone. The other twitched, a spasmodic, mechanical gesture. Its carapace was cracked. From the fissures, thin tendrils of smoke escaped into the cold air. Its lens was dark, scoured by ice.

Kiona stood. She walked toward it, her boots crunching on the new snow. She stood over the broken machine. The ghost. It was just a thing now, a collection of parts leaking their life away. Defeated not by a greater weapon, but by the consequence of its own presence, the pressure it exerted on a fragile world.

The lens flickered. A faint, dying light within it. The speaker crackled, spitting static. Then a voice, stripped of its digital smoothness, raw and fragmented.

“...anomaly… un-reconciled… debt… un…”

The light guttered out. The twitching arm fell still.

Kiona looked from the machine to the vast, reshaped scar in the land. She had not defeated it with its own tools. She had remembered the land’s language. She had listened to its fragility and let the antagonist’s own weight be its end. The ghost was defeated by the truth of its own insubstantiality against the solid, brutal fact of the world.

She felt no triumph. Only a hollow, clean exhaustion. The cold seed in her gut was gone. In its place was a quiet, steady beat. Her own heart, no longer a frantic bird trapped in a cage of ribs, but a drum. A thing that marked time, that remembered, that moved forward.

She was reborn into the silence. Not as the girl fleeing the drone at her window, not as the courier with the case, not as the witness. She was Kiona, who had walked into the white and had a conversation with the edge of the world. She was the variable that had survived its own deletion.

She turned north again. The canyon was no longer a barrier. The avalanche had created a treacherous, sloping ramp of debris down to the river. A way across. A way forward.

She began the careful, painful descent. Each step was an integration. The fear, the grief, the cold—they were not gone. They were part of the rhythm now. The drumbeat of her heart encompassed them all.

She reached the river’s edge, the water black and swift with melt. She would find a way to cross. She would keep moving. The machine-heart would send more teeth, more ghosts. But it would be chasing the story now, not just the woman. And stories, once born into the world, were harder to kill than people.

As she picked her way along the shattered shore, a glint in the water caught her eye. Not a stone. Something smooth, manufactured. A piece of the drone’s carapace, perhaps, carried down by the avalanche. She knelt, reached into the biting cold of the water, and pulled it out.

It was not drone wreckage.

It was a data-drive. A small, sealed unit of black composite, identical to the ones from the case. It must have been thrown free in the explosion at the cave, carried by the subterranean water, and spat out here, in this new violence. A lost child, surfacing.

She held it in her palm, water dripping from her numb fingers. The ghost-light pulsed within it, a faint, blue heartbeat against the black.

The thing in the deep water was not just dreaming. It was sending out messages in bottles.

She closed her fist around it. The cold of it was electric. It was no longer a payload. It was an answer. And she was no longer a courier.

She was a question.

She tucked the drive inside her parka, against her skin. The cold was a shock, then a constant. A companion. She stood, looked up at the impossible climb on the far side, and began.

Above, in the flat, watching grey of the sky, a new sensor satellite, drawn by the seismic signature of the avalanche, finished its calibration. Its unblinking eye focused on the heat-blur of a single human form, a tiny, persistent protest against the white, climbing out of the scar. A new variable, now carrying a secret that hummed with its own ghost-light. A signal in the silence, waiting to be read.

The machine-heart had a new debt to calculate.

---

 Chapter 9

 CHAPTER 9

The climb was a kind of drowning in air. Each handhold was a gasp, each ledge a shallow breath before the next pull upwards. The avalanche had scoured the mountain’s face raw, leaving a treachery of loose scree and fractured bone-rock. Kiona moved through it like a needle through torn hide, a slow, deliberate stitch. The cold of the data-drive was a brand against her sternum, a second, slower heart. It did not warm. It simply persisted, a piece of deep winter lodged inside her coat.

She crested the final ridge as the weak sun bled out behind the western peaks. The world below was not the one she had left.

The valley was a wound. The avalanche had not stopped at the river; it had leapt the gap, a white tsunami of snow and rock that had slammed into the opposite tree-line and sheared it off. The familiar path of the caribou, the stands of black spruce where she’d set snares as a girl, were gone. Buried or splintered. A raw, brown scar stretched for a kilometer, a brutal geometry of ruin. The river, choked with ice and debris, had backed up into a swollen, angry lake, its surface a chaos of grinding pans.

But at the northern edge of the destruction, where the old growth still stood sentinel, there was smoke. Not one thread, but several, braiding together in the twilight air. Council smoke. The kind you lit when the world broke and people needed to remember how to be a circle.

Her body understood before her mind. The ache in her shoulders unlocked. The vigilance in her neck, the constant strain of listening for the wasp-drone’s whine, softened by a degree. She was not coming home to a silent cabin, to a grandmother’s fearful whisper. She was coming to a fire.

The descent was faster. Her legs, rubbery from the climb, found a stubborn rhythm. She entered the tree-line, the sudden hush of snow-laden boughs a balm after the open violence of the scar. The smell of woodsmoke and boiling meat grew stronger, cutting through the clean, empty scent of snow. She followed it to the clearing behind Old Man Talir’s cabin.

They were all there. Or what was left of them. Talir, his face a map of deeper wrinkles than she remembered, turning a caribou haunch on a spit over a central fire. Martha Aviq, mending a torn parka by the light of a propane lantern, her fingers never pausing. The Denoon kids, quiet for once, stacking split wood. And in a chair pulled close to the heat, wrapped in a mountain of blankets, her grandmother. Nana’s eyes were on the flames, but they were not empty. They were waiting.

Kiona stepped into the ring of light.

All movement stopped. The spit halted its turn. The needle froze mid-stitch. The children became statues of shadow and flame. It was Talir who broke the silence, not with a word, but with a slow, deep nod. It was an acknowledgment of an arrival, not an intrusion. Martha set her mending down.

Nana looked up. Her eyes found Kiona’s, travelled over the crust of ice on her pants, the raw scrape on her cheek, the way she stood, rooted yet ready to fall. She did not smile. She opened the blankets.

Kiona went to her. She knelt in the snow by the chair, the heat of the fire on one side, the frail, electric heat of her grandmother on the other. A work-roughened hand came out and touched her hair, her cheek, as if verifying she was made of solid matter and not river-mist.

“The river spoke your name last night,” Nana said, her voice the rustle of dry leaves. “A loud word. I heard it in the stones under the house.”

“It was the mountain speaking,” Kiona said, her own voice strange to her, graveled by cold and disuse. “Giving up a tooth.”

Talir resumed turning the spit. The hiss-pop of fat hitting the coals was a domestic, holy sound. “We felt it shake the roots. Saw the white cloud swallow the sky. We thought it had taken you into its mouth.”

“It tried.” Kiona sat back on her heels, the weight of the drive against her chest a palpable secret. The ordinary world was here—the smell of meat, the pile of mending, the stacked wood—but it was perched on the edge of the new scar. It was an island. And she had brought the sea with her. “The ghosts followed me to the high pass. One of them… woke the mountain up.”

Martha Aviq picked up her needle again. “The black wasps. They have been thick these past days. Circling. Not coming close, but watching. Like crows before a storm.”

“They are looking for a story that got away from them,” Kiona said. The words felt too large for the firelight. But this was the place for large words. This circle was the oldest container they had.

Her grandmother’s hand found hers, squeezed. “You have it?”

Kiona looked at the faces, etched by firelight and a shared, unspoken understanding. They had not asked where she had been, what she had carried. They had only seen her go, and now they saw her return, marked by the mountain. They were the welcome that did not demand, the recognition that asked no proof. It was the first true warmth she had felt, and it made the cold of the drive feel like a betrayal.

“I did,” she said, the truth a half-thing, a shadow on the rock. “It’s gone. The mountain has it now.” This was not a lie. It was one layer of the story. The case was buried under a thousand tons of grief-stone. That truth was solid.

Nana watched her, eyes like chips of obsidian. She saw the shadow. She always did. But she only nodded, slowly. “Good. Let the mountain digest it. Its stomach is stronger than ours.”

The tension around the fire eased a fraction. The story was acceptable. A courier had been chased, the payload lost to an act of God. It was a clean, old ending. Talir began carving the meat, handing out slabs on pieces of bark. The children were summoned, given their share. Conversation turned, with deliberate care, to the business of survival. The caribou Talir had taken just before the slide. The state of the fish caches by the lake, now under three meters of snow-slurry. The best route to the next trapline, now that the old one was a tomb of ice.

Kiona ate. The meat was rich, life-giving. She listened. This was the rhythm, the drumbeat of community. It was real, and she clung to it. But under her parka, the drive pulsed its silent, blue heartbeat. A counter-rhythm. A secret drum.

She was not the same woman who had left. The circle held her, but she was a warped spoke. She had crossed the river at its most dangerous whisper. She had been seen by a satellite’s unblinking eye. She had held a thing that dreamed in the dark and now carried a piece of its dream. The ordinary world welcomed her, but she could no longer fully enter it. She was a question, sitting by their fire, eating their meat.

When the meal was done, and the children were sent to bed in Talir’s back room, and Martha began boiling water for pine-needle tea, Nana’s hand tightened on Kiona’s again.

“You are tired in your bones,” she stated. “But your spirit is not sleeping. It is watching a different door.”

Kiona met her gaze. She could not share the drive. To bring it into the light here would be to draw the wasps to this fire, to this circle of mending and meat. It would be the deepest debt. But she could share the weight of it. Not the object, but the shape of the burden.

“The thing I carried,” Kiona said, her voice low, for her grandmother’s ear alone. “It was not just a tł'ih’s trinket. It was… a memory. A memory of the deep earth, older than rivers. The people who sent the wasps, they wanted to bury that memory. To keep it silent.”

Nana was still, her eyes reflecting the dance of the flames. “A memory has a voice. It wants to be heard.”

“This one does,” Kiona whispered. The confession was a relief, a stone lifted from her tongue. “It spoke. And the mountain listened.”

Her grandmother was silent for a long time. The fire cracked. Across the clearing, Talir and Martha spoke in low tones about hinge-wood for a new sled.

“You did not lose all of it,” Nana said finally. It was not a question.

Kiona’s hand went, involuntarily, to her chest. The gesture was answer enough.

Nana’s breath left her in a long, slow sigh, a sound of resignation and a strange, grim pride. “A story that wants to live will find a carrier. It is a heavy gift. You cannot bring it into the house. But you cannot leave it outside in the snow. It will freeze, or it will be taken.” She looked into the fire, her face ancient and unreadable. “You must build a new house for it.”

The words settled into Kiona, making sense in a way that was beyond logic. A new house. Not here. Not in the ordinary world. But not out in the exposed wild, either. A threshold place.

“Where?” Kiona asked.

“You will know,” Nana said. “The story will show you. It is not our story. Not yet. But you are its bridge.” She turned her head, her eyes sharp. “The wasps will know you have it. Their eyes are everywhere. You must move like the river under the ice. You must be a voice they cannot catch.”

It was the sharing of the elixir, but not as she had imagined. Not a triumphant bestowal of wisdom, but a quiet, fearful conferral of a dangerous truth. The healing was not for the community—not yet. The healing was the act of speaking the secret into the space between them, of having it heard and held by the one person whose understanding was absolute. It was a transfusion of purpose. Her grandmother did not take the burden. She gave Kiona the strength to carry it.

Martha brought them tea. The ordinary world closed back in. They drank in silence, the three elders and the woman who was now something else. The talk returned to practicalities. Kiona would stay the night. In the morning, Talir would lend her his good snowshoes; hers were lost to the mountain. They would give her what supplies they could spare.

It was a sending-off, but not a banishment. It was an acknowledgment that her path now diverged from the well-trodden circle around the fire. She had a different rhythm to walk to.

Later, in the loft under a pile of furs, Kiona lay awake. The drive was on the floor beside her, wrapped in a scrap of waxed canvas. Its faint pulse was invisible, but she could feel it, a vibration in the floorboards. A signal in the silence.

She was no longer a courier. The Company’s debt was a ledger entry in a machine-heart far away, a cold calculation of assets lost. She was a question. And now, she was a bridge. The new house for the story had to be built, a place where a deep-earth memory could be heard without bringing the sky down on the people by the fire.

Her mind turned, not south towards the Company’s enclaves, but north. To the old places. The listening posts that had gone silent not with a bang, but with a slow, frozen sigh. To the ghost-towns of the last century’s fever-dream, where the wires were dead but the structures might still stand. A place in the ruins, where a new signal could be hidden among the echoes of old ones.

The satellite had seen her climb out of the scar. It would be tracking her heat-signature, predicting her vector. It would expect her to flee to population, to cover. It would calculate her debt and her fear.

So she would not flee. She would go deeper. Into the white silence where the machine-heart’s models grew thin, where the old maps said here there be giants. She would become a glitch in its vision, a persistent, inexplicable heat-blur moving towards the cold, dark heart of the forgotten world. She would build her new house from the bones of the old one.

Outside, the wind picked up, moaning through the shattered valley. It was a clean sound, a scouring sound. It carried away the scent of woodsmoke and meat, replacing it with the endless, empty breath of the north.

Kiona closed her eyes. The ghost-light of the drive, muffled in canvas, pulsed against her closed lids. A message in a bottle. And she was the current that would carry it to a shore no one was watching.

In the flat, watching grey of the sky, the sensor satellite traced the stationary heat-source clustered at the coordinates of Talir’s cabin. It logged the presence of multiple human signatures. It identified one as a 78% match for the variable designated Kiona-747. The machine-heart noted the convergence, calculated the increased metabolic and social probability of the variable remaining stationary for 12-48 hours. It adjusted its predictive algorithms. It tasked two Reaper-class drones, currently monitoring seismic activity in Sector Gamma, to divert and establish a high-altitude, low-observability watch perimeter around the location.

The variable was dormant. The debt was stationary. The equation was balancing.

The machine-heart did not dream of deep water, or of memories in stone. It dreamed of closure. Of neat lines connecting cause to effect, asset to loss, variable to zero. It sent its silent, patient ghosts to ring the firelight, to wait for the variable to move, to re-enter the stream of logic.

It waited for the story to make sense.

And deep beneath the ice, in the dark where the pressure shaped time into new forms, the dreamer stirred. A fragment of itself had surfaced. It had been found. The carrier was moving. The signal was in the world. It was a weak pulse, a single neuron firing in a vast, cold body. But it was a beginning.

It sent out another pulse, not a memory this time, but a resonance. A tuning fork struck in the abyss. It traveled through the bedrock, a subtle harmonic, seeking the signature of its own lost piece.

The stone beneath Talir’s cabin hummed, a note too low for human ears.

Kiona, on the edge of sleep, felt it in her teeth.

---

 Chapter 10

 CHAPTER 10

The hum was in the walls.

Kiona woke with it, a vibration in the marrow of her jaw, a pressure behind her eyes. It wasn’t a sound. It was the ghost of a sound, the memory of a frequency that had already passed through the log walls, the stone foundation, the flesh and bone of her sleeping body. She lay on the floor by the cooling stove, wrapped in a caribou hide, and stared at the smoke-stained ceiling. The dreamer in the deep was tuning itself. And she was the reed.

Across the room, Talir snored softly, a ragged, human rhythm against the subsonic thrum. Her grandmother was a still mound under blankets on the cot, but Kiona could see her eyes were open, reflecting the dull red embers through the stove’s grate. Watching the dark.

The drone-light was gone from the window. The silence outside was a different kind of noise—the held breath of something waiting. The machine-heart had drawn its circle. She’d felt its logic like a cold draft when the satellite’s eye had passed over them last night, a statistical gaze that pinned them like specimens. Dormant variable. Stationary debt. She’d carried the drive here, a splinter of ancient memory, and in doing so, she had made this cabin a target. She had painted a circle around everyone she had left.

The practical work was a relief. It was a knife to cut the eerie tension. At first light, she banked the fire, melted snow for water. The hum had faded, or she had grown accustomed to it, a new permanent ringing in the silence of her head. She checked the perimeter, not with her eyes, but with her body, listening for the absence of natural sound—the gap where a bird call should be, the hollow space in the wind where a Reaper’s rotors weren’t. She found nothing. That was the point. The ghosts were polite. They didn’t rattle the windows. They just made the world outside feel like a diorama behind glass.

Talir stirred when the smell of pine needles and boiling water filled the cabin. He sat up, his face creased and old in the grey dawn. He looked at Kiona, then at the old woman on the cot. No greeting. Just a slow, assessing nod. He saw the hum in her, too.

“The water speaks today,” he said, his voice gravelly with sleep.

Her grandmother turned her head on the pillow. “It is not the water. It is the bone of the world. It aches.”

“The drive,” Kiona said, the word too sharp, too modern in the close, smoky air. She pulled the canvas-wrapped brick from her inner pocket, where it had pressed against her ribs all night. It was warm. Not from her body heat. A chemical warmth, like a stone left in the sun. “It’s… responding. To something down there. Or something down there is responding to it.”

Talir held out a hand. She placed the bundle in his palm. He didn’t unwrap it. He held it, his eyes closed, his head tilted as if listening to a shell. After a long minute, he opened his eyes. “It is a piece of a story. A story that broke. The break is sharp. It cuts both ways.” He handed it back. “You cannot keep a splinter in your flesh. It will work its way in, or work its way out.”

“I have to get it to the coast. To the Annie M.” She named the old supply trawler that still made the risky spring run up to Tulu Bay. “Marlow might still be crazy enough to make the trip.”

“Marlow is dead,” her grandmother said from the cot, matter-of-fact. “His son runs the boat now. He has a machine that listens to the sky. He calls it insurance.”

A son. A new machine. The world kept updating itself, leaving older, simpler versions behind. Kiona felt a surge of useless anger. “Then I’ll find another way.”

“The ghosts outside will find it first,” Talir said. He rose, stiffly, and poured himself a cup of the pine-needle tea. “You moved fast, alone. You were a single fish in a big river. Now you are a stone in a pool. The ripples are easy to see. They,” he nodded toward the window, toward the empty sky, “they like things that stay still. It makes the math quiet.”

The first scene of her new reality was this: the three of them, trapped in a cabin by invisible walls of data and prediction. The victory of the night before—the sharing of the memory, the solid, undeniable truth of it—was now a liability. The convergence the machine-heart had logged. She had to un-converge. She had to break the pattern its logic was so comfortably settling into.

“We need to scatter,” she said. “Like ptarmigan in a hawk’s shadow. You go north to the ridges, Grandmother. Talir, you have that line cabin up on the Black Knife. Go. Be separate heat signatures. Be moving variables.”

Her grandmother pushed herself up on one elbow. Her eyes were fierce. “You think I run from tł'ih? This is my home. My bones are in this ground.”

“Your bones won’t do you any good if a hellfire missile turns this cabin into a hole,” Kiona snapped, the fear making her voice cruel. She saw the old woman flinch, and hated herself. She softened her tone. “It’s not running. It’s… changing the story. The story they are telling about us right now is about three people hiding with a secret. We need to give them three different stories to tell.”

Talir sipped his tea. “She is not wrong, Elna. The young wolf understands the trap. She smells the steel.”

He used her grandmother’s name, a sound Kiona had almost forgotten. Elna. It made her real in a new way, separate from grandmother, a person with a history that didn’t include Kiona at all. Elna glared at him, then sank back onto the pillow, conceding. “I will go to Mary Aklok’s. She is old and her stove smokes. Their seeing-machine will think it is just a fire that is always lit. A story of nothing.”

It was a good idea. A clever corruption of the data-stream. A smudge on the lens.

“And you?” Talir asked Kiona.

“I become a fish again. I move. Tonight.” She looked at the drive in her hand. The warm, sleeping splinter. “But I need a decoy. Something for their ghosts to follow if they get impatient.”

Talir’s eyes, deep-set and dark, gleamed. He set down his cup. “I have an idea for a story they will like very much.”

The second scene was carved with knives and silence.

Talir worked on the decoy at the table, his big, scarred hands moving with a surgeon’s precision. He didn’t use modern tools. He used an ulu, its half-moon blade sharp enough to part hair. He used a length of old copper wire, a strip of waterproof canvas from his pack, a lump of pine pitch he warmed by the stove.

Kiona watched, mesmerized. He wasn’t making a replica of the drive. He was making a story of the drive. A story the machine-heart would recognize. He took a stone from the hearth—a smooth, river-worn piece of granite the size of his palm—and wrapped it tightly in the canvas. He sealed the seams with pitch. He embedded the copper wire in the pitch, leaving a short, stiff protrusion that looked like a crude data-port. He weighted the bundle with a lead fishing sinker, so it had the right heft. Then, with the ulu’s point, he etched a tiny, meticulous pattern into the hardened pitch. It looked like circuitry. It looked like the frost feathers on a windowpane. It was a perfect cipher—technological enough to trigger recognition algorithms, organic enough to be plausible as something ancient and strange.

“They see from so far away,” he murmured, not looking up. “They see heat, shape, mass. They see patterns. They do not see this.” He tapped his temple. “They do not see a lie told by an old man at a table.”

It was a act of profound subversion. It was a virus made of pitch and stone. Kiona felt a swell of something like awe. This was the deepening. Talir was no longer just a keeper of old memories; he was an active forger in the war of narratives. He was writing a false page for the machine-heart’s ledger.

Her grandmother, Elna, was packing a small bag with a quiet fury. She folded a change of clothes, a packet of hardtack, her own tin cup. She moved around Kiona like a weather system, radiating a potent mix of fear, pride, and resentment. The loose end between them wasn’t the past anymore—it was the future. A future Kiona was dictating.

Finally, Elna stopped in front of her. “You carry a piece of the deep in your pocket,” she said, her voice low. “And you think you are leading it. But maybe it is leading you. Maybe it called you to the river. Maybe it called the tł'ih to your window. You are a woman, not a current. You can choose to put the stone down.”

“And do what?” Kiona asked, her throat tight. “Let it be lost again? Let the story be only theirs? You saw. You felt it. That memory is… it’s a truth. It’s a person. Not a thing.”

Elna’s hand came up, quick as a bird, and gripped Kiona’s wrist. Her fingers were strong, the skin papery and cool. “A person who lived and died under that ice a thousand lifetimes ago. Their truth is not more important than your life. My life.” She let go, but her eyes held on. “You think you are saving a story. I think you are feeding one to a mouth that is always hungry. The machine does not care if the story is true or false. It only cares that it can be consumed. Made into a number.”

It was the clearest articulation of the anxiety that had lived in Kiona’s gut since the drone first hovered at her window. The fear wasn’t of surveillance, or of capture. It was of assimilation. Of having the wild, wet, singing truth of the memory—of the river, of the dreamer, of her own journey—processed into a quiet, stationary data-point. A closed ticket.

“I have to try,” Kiona said, and it sounded feeble, childish.

Elna sighed, the anger leaving her like a tide going out, leaving only a vast, weary shore. She reached into the neck of her dress and pulled out a thin leather cord. Hanging from it was a small, carved piece of ivory—a diving loon, its lines worn smooth by generations of skin. “My mother gave me this. It is for seeing clearly in dark water.” She looped it over Kiona’s head. The loon rested cold against Kiona’s sternum. “Do not just look. See.”

It was a redefinition. Not a blessing, but a tool. An allyship forged not in agreement, but in the stark, clean love of a warning given. Kiona nodded, unable to speak. The hum in the walls seemed to swell for a moment, agreeing.

By late afternoon, the pieces were set. Talir’s decoy was complete, a dark, enigmatic lump that seemed to suck the light from the room. He had also fashioned a second, identical pouch from scrap leather, empty. “For the true one,” he said. “A twin. Confusion is a good friend.”

Kiona transferred the warm drive to the empty pouch, feeling its resonant heat through the leather. She put it back inside her jacket. The decoy went into an outer pocket.

The plan was simple, brutal in its geometry. At twilight, Elna would leave first, heading east toward Mary Aklok’s, a journey of a few hours on snowshoes. An hour later, Talir would leave, heading northwest into the rugged hills, carrying the decoy in a prominent place on his pack. He would move fast, leaving a clear thermal trail, a beacon of interesting data. Kiona would wait until full dark, then slip out and head due south, toward the coast and the hope of a boat.

They ate a last meal of stew in silence. The cabin, which had felt like a sanctuary, now felt like a cell counting down to its own abandonment. The fire popped. The wind picked up, keening under the eaves. The machine-heart’s ghosts were out there, listening to the weather, listening to their heartbeats through the walls.

Elna left as the sun bled out along the western ridges. She did not look back. She became a small, dark, determined shape, swallowed by the blue shadows of the spruce. Kiona watched from the window until there was nothing to watch.

Talir checked his gear, his face a mask of concentration. “The decoy will sing to them,” he said. “It has a… smell. The copper, the pitch. Their sniffers will taste it on the wind from five miles up. They will like my story better than yours. It is louder.” He hoisted his pack. At the door, he paused. “The hum you feel. The dreamer. It is not calling you, Kiona-747.” He used the variable name without irony, a cold label. “It is calling its piece home. You are the path. Remember that. A path can be walked, or it can be washed away. Be the walking.”

Then he was gone, too, and the cabin was hollow.

Kiona was alone with the ticking stove and the subsonic pulse in her teeth. She was the dormant variable, about to reactivate. The equation was about to be unbalanced. She felt a strange, electric clarity. This was the shattering. Not a battle, but a fragmentation. A single, clear story—the girl with the drive in the cabin—was splitting into three divergent, messy, human tales. It was a denial of the machine-heart’s deepest desire: for a neat, convergent solution.

She waited in the growing dark, feeling the true drive against her ribs. It was warmer than before. It seemed to beat in time with the low thrum from below. A tuning fork struck in the abyss. She was carrying the struck note. And somewhere, the great, waiting body of the dreamer was listening for its echo.

Full dark. The third scene began with a silent door and a colder wind.

Kiona slipped out, leaving the stove to die behind her. She carried nothing but a small daypack with a little food, the water bottle, and the knife. She wore white, an old snow-smock of Talir’s that smelled of smoke and man. She was a ghost against the snow, moving south, using the starless gloom of the tree-line for cover.

For the first hour, the world was merely winter. The crunch of her snowshoes, the saw of her own breath, the immense, indifferent sky. She listened for the wrong sound—the electric whine of a quad rotor, the faint Doppler whimper of a satellite passing overhead. She heard nothing but the forest settling into its night bones.

She made good time, her body falling into the old, long-distance rhythm. The hum was a constant companion, a private frequency only she could hear. She thought of Elna, arriving at Mary Aklok’s, telling some cover-story about a quarrel with Talir. She thought of Talir, moving fast and high, a dark beacon on a thermal scan, leading a chorus of ghosts on a wild goose chase across the knife-edge ridges. She hoped his story was a good one. She hoped the machine-heart found it utterly compelling.

She crested a low rise and paused in the lee of a granite outcrop. Below her lay a wide, frozen muskeg, a plain of white under the vague ambient light of the cloud-choked sky. The direct route south went straight across it. Two miles of exposure.

The anxiety was a chemical taste on her tongue. Technology’s gaze loved open ground. It was a clean, well-lit room for its algorithms. The forest was messy, full of false positives—animals, shifting snow, heat-sinks in rocks. The muskeg was a spreadsheet.

She could go around, adding hours. Or she could go across, a swift, silent dart through the open field of its vision.

She chose the field. It was an act of defiance. A gamble that its eyes were elsewhere, fixed on Talir’s louder, more interesting signal. She took a deep breath, adjusted her pack, and stepped out from the trees.

The silence on the muskeg was absolute. Deeper than in the woods. Her snowshoes made a soft, rhythmic swish-crunch, swish-crunch. She felt like the only moving particle in a vast, still experiment. Halfway across, the hum in her teeth intensified. Not a pulse, but a sustained note, rising in pitch. The drive against her chest grew hot, almost uncomfortably so. She clutched it through her layers, fearing it would glow, would become a beacon in the infrared.

Then she saw the light.

Not in the sky. On the ice. A soft, greenish-blue shimmer, emanating from the frozen surface of a shallow pond to her left. It pulsed in time with the hum. It was the same light from the memory-vision—the dreamer’s light. It was here. A seepage. A response to the fragment she carried.

She froze, mesmerized and horrified. It was beautiful. It was a catastrophic signature. Any spectral sensor, any low-light camera would flag it as an anomaly. A bloom of bioluminescence in a frozen sub-arctic muskeg was a screaming outlier in the data-set.

She had to move. Now. She broke into a clumsy, snowshoeing run, veering away from the glowing pond.

From above, and from nowhere, came a sound. Not the whine of a drone. A soft, profound thump. Like a giant heart beating once, deep in the earth.

The ice beneath her feet shuddered. Not a crack, but a wave. A single, rolling tremor that traveled through the frozen mat of grass and peat and water. It knocked her off her feet. She landed hard on her side, the breath driven from her lungs.

The hum in her teeth spiked into a silent scream. The light from the pond flared, then died abruptly.

And then she heard the other sound. The one she had been waiting for and dreading. A sound like a giant, mechanical mosquito. Distant, but closing fast. From the north. From the direction Talir had gone.

They hadn’t taken his bait. Or they had, and they’d left a ghost to watch the empty stage anyway. And she had just lit a flare on it.

She scrambled up, her heart a frantic drum against the drive’s heat. She was a sitting duck on the white plain. The tree-line on the far side was a dark smudge, a mile away. The thump-whine of the drone was louder, triangulating.

Then, a new light. Not from below. From above. A thin, focused beam of white light, piercing the clouds from a great height. It didn’t sweep. It pinned. It painted a perfect, dazzling circle on the ice twenty yards to her right. A spotlight from a Reaper flying at the edge of the stratosphere. It wasn’t trying to illuminate her. It was marking her. For the smaller, quicker drone. Or for something else.

The message was clear: We see you. You are not hidden. You are a variable in a lit circle.

This was the new threat. Not pursuit, but designation. She was no longer a blip to be tracked. She was a target to be contained. The technological anxiety crystallized into a perfect, diamond-hard truth: the system didn’t need to chase you. It just needed to paint you, and let your own panic, your own desperate movement, tell it everything it needed to know.

She did the only thing she could. She stopped running. She stood still in the white smock, in the vast white field, under the white, piercing eye from heaven. She became a statue. She let her breathing slow. She let the cold seep into her bones. She was a rock. A snow-drift. A stationary, uninteresting thing.

The spotlight held its circle on the ice, unwavering. The smaller drone’s whine circled somewhere in the dark behind the clouds, confused. The logic was disrupted. The variable had stopped. The story had halted.

Deep in the earth, the dreamer sent another pulse. This one was different. Not a hum, but a command. A single, resonant note that seemed to bypass her ears and speak directly to the ancient, reptile part of her brain.

Down.

Without thought, Kiona dropped. Not to the ice, but into a shallow, snow-filled depression beside a frozen tussock. She burrowed, pulling snow over herself, making herself part of the landscape’s texture. The drive was a furnace against her skin.

The spotlight circle quivered, then began to move. It slid across the ice, searching, probing. It passed over her depression. The light was blinding even through the snow. It paused. She held her breath, her body screaming to run. The loon carving felt like ice on her chest.

Then, from the far tree-line—Talir’s direction—a brilliant flash of orange light. A sudden, roaring bloom of heat. A fire. A huge one. Talir’s story had just gotten explosive.

The spotlight snapped away from her, racing across the muskeg toward the new, urgent, data-rich signal. The smaller drone’s whine changed pitch and shot away toward the fire.

Kiona didn’t wait. She erupted from the snow and ran, not with panic, but with a terrible, focused grace. The tree-line rushed to meet her. She hit the dark wall of spruce and dove into it, crashing through branches, not caring about noise now, only about depth, about getting lost in the messy, chaotic, living data of the woods.

She ran until her lungs were strips of torn leather. Then she collapsed against a tree trunk, gulping air, listening.

Silence. The spotlight was gone. The drones were gone, drawn to Talir’s magnificent, sacrificial fiction. He had given her a door. He had set himself on fire to do it.

She touched the drive through her clothes. It was cooling. The hum was gone. In its place was a new sensation: a pull. A gentle, insistent tugging, like a compass needle finding north. But this north was not on any map. It was downward, and south-southwest. Toward the coast, yes. But toward a specific point on the coast. The dreamer wasn’t just calling its piece home anymore. It was giving her directions.

The status quo had not just changed. It had inverted. She was no longer a carrier on a map of her own choosing. She was a needle being drawn to a magnet in the deep. The machine-heart had its stories—of a fire in the hills, of a stationary debt, of a variable momentarily lost. But beneath its logic, beneath the ice and the stone, a older, slower story was re-knitting itself. And she was the thread.

She got to her feet. She adjusted the pack. She felt the cool ivory loon against her skin. See clearly in dark water.

She looked south, into the tangled, breathing dark of the forest. The hook was set, not in her, but in the world. She had shattered the machine’s expectation, but in doing so, she had stepped fully into a different, deeper plot. The path was walking her now.

She moved forward, following the new, silent pull in her blood. Above, in the absolute black, the sensor satellite logged the extinguished thermal anomaly on the muskeg, and the new, raging thermal event in Sector Theta. It adjusted its probabilities. It noted the variable designated Kiona-747 was once again lost to resolution. The story, from its perspective, was once again senseless.

And deep below, in the crushing, patient dark, the dreamer felt the fragment draw closer. It sent a pulse of gratitude through the stone. It was a vibration of continents shifting. It was the first word of a sentence ten thousand years long.

Kiona felt it in the roots of her teeth, and knew, with a certainty that was colder than the ice and older than the river, that she would never be free of it again.

---

 Chapter 11

 CHAPTER 11

The world had a new gravity and Kiona was a stone in its pull.

She walked for two days in the forest’s deep hush, a silence that was not an absence but a listening. The trees, the moss, the cold black pools—they were all leaning into the same low hum she felt in her marrow. The pull was a clean, quiet line through the chaos of slope and thicket. It bypassed the old traplines, ignored the game trails her feet knew by heart. It was a path of intention, not geography. She was not navigating. She was being navigated.

She slept in the lee of spruce roots, the ivory loon a cold comfort against her chest. She dreamed not of the machine-heart’s frantic glyphs, but of weight. A mountain on her back. The slow, granular slide of a continent into the sea. She woke with her jaw aching, as if she’d been clenching it for hours against a pressure only her bones could sense.

On the third morning, the forest began to fray at its edges. The scent of woodsmoke and stale grease threaded through the clean smell of pine.
