Tomas moved quickly. He scrabbled up the pile, grabbed the exposed end of the staff, and pulled. It was lodged fast. He took a rock and hammered at it where it met the rubble, striking with furious, focused blows. On the fifth strike, the black material cracked with a sound like snapping bone. He wrenched the broken piece free. It was about the length of his forearm, jagged at one end. The rest remained buried, a dead nerve in the stone.

He held the fragment, staring at it. It was inert. No light, no hum. Just a piece of strange, lightweight alloy, cold to the touch. A relic.

“We keep this,” he said, his voice low. “We may need to understand what we are fighting.”

“We need to go,” Jiro said, his eyes scanning the northern horizon. “The specialized unit it called for… it may still come, to investigate the silence.”

The practicality of it got them moving. They gathered their scattered packs, checked on the few who had been bruised by flying rock. The fear had not left; it had simply been compressed, made portable. They shouldered it along with their gear.

As they prepared to move out, Tomas approached Kiona. She was wiping the blood from her face with a handful of snow. He held out a strip of dried caribou meat. “Eat,” he said. The command was softer than before.

She took it. Her hands were trembling.

“What you did…” he started, then stopped, choosing his words with visible care. “It was not of this world. But it saved us a span of time. A gift. Use it wisely.”

“It felt wrong,” she said, the words escaping her before she could cage them.

“Good,” he said, and there was no cruelty in it, only a bleak honesty. “It should. We are using tools we do not understand to fight things we understand even less. Wrong is the only direction left to us.” He looked north, toward the path that wound along the frozen river. “The song you carry… it is waking up. And it is making you into a weapon. We must hope the weapon knows who to point at.”

He walked away to help an older woman with her pack.

They moved out within the hour, a ragged line of thirty souls against the vast, white land. The scar on the bluff and the silent rubble pile grew smaller behind them, a wound in the afternoon. Kiona walked at the center of the group, not by design, but because the others unconsciously positioned her there. A resource to be protected. A danger to be contained.

She felt the weight of the broken staff fragment in Tomas’s pack as if it were in her own. A dead tongue that had almost sung their doom. And she felt the hook, quiet now, a sleeping, fitted piece in the cavity of her chest. It had learned a new trick. It had learned to lie.

They made camp at dusk in a stand of thick spruce that offered some shelter from the biting wind. The fire was small, hidden, its smoke dissipated through the dense needles. People huddled around it, passing a single pot of thin broth made from melted snow and a precious crumble of dried meat. No one spoke much. The events of the day were too large for words; they sat in the stomach like undigested stones.

Kiona found a spot at the edge of the firelight, her back against a tree. She was exhausted down to her marrow, but sleep felt like a distant country. She watched the flames, their dance a simpler, cleaner song than any she had touched today.

Old Man Ben settled beside her, groaning softly as he lowered himself to the ground. He offered her a piece of hardtack. She took it, nibbling without appetite.

“Your grandmother,” he said after a while, his eyes on the fire. “She knows the old stories. The ones from before the machines went quiet.”

Kiona nodded.

“There is a story,” Ben said, his voice a low rumble. “About the Tełtthąą, the One Who Remembers Wrong.”

Kiona looked at him. She knew some of the old stories, but not this one.

“It was not a bad spirit,” Ben continued. “It was just… broken. It would come into a camp and see a mother holding her child, and it would remember that scene as a warrior sharpening a knife. It would hear a love song and remember it as a death chant. It would take the true stories of the world and twist them in its memory, and then it would tell the twisted versions back to the people. After a while, people did not know what was true anymore. They began to live inside the wrong stories. They became afraid of their own children. They heard threats in lullabies.”

He poked the fire with a stick. “The people had to drive it away. Not because it was evil, but because its broken memory was a poison to their own. They sang the true stories, loud and clear, for seven days and nights, until the Tełtthąą could no longer hold its wrong versions against the truth. It faded, back into the places between places.”

Ben turned his old, watery eyes to her. “You put a wrong story into that machine today. You made it remember itself as broken.”

A chill that had nothing to do with the wind went through Kiona. “You think I am like that spirit?”

“I think,” Ben said slowly, “the tool in your chest is from the world that made those machines. It speaks their language. And today, you used it to tell a lie in that language. This is a new path. A dangerous one. The hook may be learning how to lie, too. And if it starts remembering things wrong… whose stories will you be telling then?”

He patted her knee, a dry, papery touch, and pushed himself up to seek his bedroll.

Kiona sat alone, Ben’s story coiling in her mind like smoke. She placed a hand over her chest. The hook was silent, a dormant seed. Had she taught it deception? Was it now a Tełtthąą in her own blood, waiting to twist the true songs of the land—of the river, of the ice, of the sleeping machine—into something false and usable?

She looked up through the spruce boughs at the few hard stars visible between the clouds. The green pulse was gone, but the air itself felt watchful. The specialized unit was still out there, perhaps homing in on the last known coordinates, finding only silence and rubble. They would investigate. They would find the broken staff. They would know its signal had been artificially terminated. They would know the “anomalous signal” had the capability to deceive.

She had bought them distance, but she had also shown a new card in a game she didn’t understand the rules of. The listeners were not just multiplying. They were learning what she could do.

A soft crunch of snow made her turn. Jiro stood there, his bandaged hand held against his stomach. His face was pale in the gloom.

“It’s back,” he whispered.

For a heart-stopping moment, she thought he meant the green pulse. But he was pointing north, not south.

“The call. The one the staff was answering. It’s fainter. Different. But it’s there.” He swallowed. “It’s not from the pipeline anymore. It’s from farther north. From where we’re going. And it’s… asking a question.”

Kiona stood, her fatigue forgotten. “What question?”

Jiro’s eyes were wide, unfocused, listening to something only the poison in his hand could hear. “It’s asking for a status update. But the code… it’s older. Deeper. It’s not calling to the staff. It’s calling to the thing the staff was made to guard.” He looked at her, true terror in his face for the first time. “It’s calling to the Sleeper. And it’s using a word I haven’t heard before.”

“What word?”

Jiro’s lips moved soundlessly, then found the shape. “Catalyst. It is asking if the Catalyst is active.”

The fire popped, sending a spiral of sparks into the dark. Around them, the camp slept on, innocent of the new thread being pulled taut in the night. Kiona looked north, into the blackness beyond the trees. The river was there, under the ice, telling its long story. And now, something at the source of it was awake enough to ask a new, specific question.

It was asking for her.

The hook in her chest gave a single, quiet, resonant thrum.

Yes.

---

 Chapter 16

 CHAPTER 16

The word hung in the air between them, a new kind of cold. Catalyst. It did not sound like a thing to be. It sounded like a thing to be used. Kiona felt the hook in her chest go still and silent again, a listening silence. The fire’s crackle was obscene in its normalcy.

Jiro was shaking, a fine tremor that started in his bandaged hand and climbed his arm. “It’s not a voice,” he said, his own voice thin with the effort of explanation. “It’s a… protocol. Buried in the carrier wave. A repeating query. Status of primary containment. Status of the Sleeper. Is the Catalyst active? It’s old, Kiona. Older than the pipeline signal. It’s like… bedrock code.”

Kiona moved to the fire, not for warmth but for the light. She needed to see his face. “The staff was answering it. Reporting.”

“Yes. Sending a steady ‘all-nominal’ signal back along the same band. A heartbeat. My… modification,” he glanced at his hand, “it must have mimicked that heartbeat when I boosted the jamming pulse. It got the old system’s attention. Now the heartbeat is gone. It’s asking why.”

She poured him a cup of bitter spruce tea from the pot hanging over the flames. Her hands were steady. Her grandmother’s hands, steady when gutting a fish, steady when lighting the seal-oil lamp in a gale. “Drink. Then tell me everything you can about the signal. Direction. Strength. Interval.”

He took the cup, sloshing some over the rim. “North-northwest. Stronger than the pipeline signal ever was, but… deeper. Lower frequency. It’s not broadcasting to the sky. It’s pushing through the ground, I think. Using the permafrost as a waveguide. The interval is every seventeen minutes, exactly.”

“A sentry round,” Kiona murmured. She thought of the stories, the old ones about the Tunit, the people before the people, who left stone figures to watch the coasts. They were said to turn their heads, once every tide, to check for danger. A slow, patient vigilance. “What happens if no one answers?”

Jiro met her eyes. “I don’t know. It might escalate. Send a different kind of pulse. Or it might… dispatch something.”

The camp was a shallow bowl of stillness around them. Mari and Ansel slept bundled together under a shared hide. Old Ben’s snores sawed the air from his lean-to. They were trusting her to hear the things they couldn’t. She had heard the river’s chuckle under the ice. Now she was hearing the mountain’s query under the stone.

“We move at first light,” she said, her voice low and final. “Faster. No more than four hours rest a night. We follow that call.”

“To do what?” Jiro’s question was not a challenge, but a plea for a map where there was none.

“To answer it,” Kiona said. And then, because the truth was the only tool she had left, she added, “Before something else does.”

---

The land began to change on the third day of the forced march. The dense, sheltering spruce forest thinned, giving way to vast, rolling hummocks of tundra, snow-swept and open to a sky that seemed to press down like a lid of hammered lead. The wind here had no obstacles. It spoke in a single, relentless vowel, a sound that hollowed out the ears and seeped into the brain. They traveled now in a tight, silent file, heads down against the blast, feeling the exposed stretch of their own spines.

The river was their only guide. It had shrunk, cutting a deeper, darker channel through the permanent ice of the valley floor. Its talk was different here—not a chuckle, but a low, grinding grumble, the sound of great weight moving with infinite patience. It was the sound of the glacier it sprang from, still fifty klicks north.

Jiro walked just behind Kiona, his good hand cradling the bandaged one against his chest. He had become their dowsing rod. Every seventeen minutes, he would stiffen, his head tilting as if catching a distant whistle. He no longer announced it. Kiona could see it in the hitch of his shoulders, the way his eyes lost focus, turning inward to the private frequency only he could suffer. After a minute, he would relax, a fraction, and give her a single, grim nod. Still asking.

On the afternoon of the fourth day, they found the bones.

It was Mari who saw them first, her hunter’s eyes catching the wrong white against the snow. Not stone, not ice. She held up a mittened hand, and the line halted.

They were caribou, a small herd of perhaps ten. But they were not scattered by wolves or collapsed from starvation. They lay in a rough circle, their skeletons picked perfectly clean, not a shred of tendon or hide left. The bones were pure, almost luminous against the grey snow, and they were cold. Kiona knelt, removed her mitten, and held her palm an inch above a rib. A deep, aching cold radiated from it, a cold that had nothing to do with the air.

“No marks,” Ansel said, his voice hushed. He prodded a skull with his rifle barrel. “No bullet holes, no cracks. It’s like they just… lay down and let the flesh fall off.”

Old Ben shuffled forward, his breath puffing rapid clouds. He didn’t touch the bones. He stared at the pattern they made. “Circle,” he grunted. “Not a sleeping circle. A receiving circle.”

Kiona looked at him. “Receiving what?”

Ben’s milky eyes scanned the empty tundra, the low sky. “A strong signal. It cooks from the inside. Seen it before, long time back. Near the old DEW Line station they abandoned. A wolf pack. Same thing. Clean bones, cold as space.” He tapped his own chest. “Messes with the water inside. Makes it boil in the blood.”

Jiro had gone very still. He was staring north, along the river’s course. “The carrier wave,” he whispered. “If it’s using the ground… it could have local peaks. Antinodes. Like a standing wave in a pond.” He looked at the bones, then at his bandaged hand. “A high-gain area. They stood here when the query pulse came through.”

A new kind of fear settled on the group, colder and quieter than the fear of drones or soldiers. This was a land that could kill you by asking a question too loudly. Kiona stood, her knees cracking. “We give this place a wide berth. Follow the riverbank, but stay off the high ground. Move.”

As they skirted the circle of bones, Kiona felt the hook in her chest give a faint, curious tremor. Not a pull. An echo. As if it had felt the afterimage of the pulse that had felled the caribou and was… analyzing it.

---

That night, they camped in the lee of a massive, weathered erratic, a house-sized boulder dropped by the retreating glacier millennia ago. It blocked the worst of the wind, but the cold was a penetrating, mineral thing that seeped up from the earth itself. They dared not light a fire that could be seen for miles in the flat expanse. They huddled in their hides, chewing on frozen strips of fish and fat, their breath mingling in a shared cloud.

Jiro sat apart, his back against the stone, his eyes closed. The seventeen-minute cycle was a clockwork torture. Each time, he flinched.

Kiona brought him his food. He opened his eyes. They were red-rimmed, the sockets bruised with exhaustion. “It’s changing,” he said, without preamble. “The query. It’s added a new subroutine. A… diagnostic.”

She sat beside him, the cold of the rock immediately bleeding through her layers. “Explain.”

“After the main question—Is the Catalyst active?—there’s a secondary burst now. A scan. It’s very short-range. Maybe only a few hundred meters.” He swallowed. “I think it’s looking for the staff. Or for something with the same… signature.”

Kiona’s blood slowed. “Your hand.”

He gave a single, jerky nod. “It’s reading the residue. The nano-fluid. Every time the pulse goes out, I can feel it… resonate. Like a tuning fork being struck.” He held up the bandaged lump. “It’s not just listening anymore, Kiona. It’s pinging me back.”

The implications unfolded in her mind, cold and logical. They were following a beacon that was now, in a faint and sickly way, beaconing back. They were a blur on the edge of a slow, patient radar.

“Can you block it? Muffle it?”

“I don’t know. It’s not a transmitter. It’s a… a stain. The pulse excites it.” He looked desperate. “I could try to shield it. Lead would be best. But we don’t have any.”

Kiona looked across the small camp to where Old Ben was meticulously greasing his boots with a dab of precious fat. She stood and walked over to him.

“Ben. The old DEW Line station. The one where you saw the wolves. How far?”

The old man squinted into the memory. “East. Two days, maybe three, for us now. It’s a ghost place. They took the big machines, left the shells. Why?”

“They would have had shielding there. For their machines. Lead sheets. Lead-lined rooms.”

Ben’s face wrinkled in understanding, then doubt. “Maybe. Scavengers have been through there for fifty years. Might be nothing but bird shit and rust.”

“It’s a chance.” She returned to Jiro. “We divert. East. Two days. We find lead, we shield your hand. Then we turn north again.”

Jiro’s face flooded with a pathetic, painful hope. Then it collapsed. “Two days there. Two days back. That’s four days lost. The query pulse is changing now. In four days, it might escalate to something we can’t hide from. Or it might… send a physical response.”

He was right. It was a gamble with time they didn’t have. Kiona felt the decision settle on her, heavy and absolute. There were no good paths, only paths that kept you moving.

“Then we don’t go back,” she said. “We go east to the station, then cut north from there. It’s longer. Harder. But we get the shielding, and we stay ahead of the query’s source.” She looked at the others, who had gathered close, drawn by the low, urgent tension. Mari, Ansel, Ben. Their faces were gaunt, etched with the cold and the relentless travel. They said nothing. They waited for her order. The weight of it was a stone in her throat. “We go east at first light.”

Mari simply nodded. Ansel checked the action of his rifle, a mute affirmation. Old Ben sighed, a long, weary stream of vapour. “East it is. The land is meaner that way. Broken. Old ice.”

It was the closest he would come to an objection. Kiona accepted it. “Then we’ll be meaner too.”

---

The broken land began by midday. The smooth tundra buckled into a terrain of pressure ridges and deep, snow-filled fissures. They were walking on the skin of a giant, restless body. The river vanished into a canyon to their left, its grumble now a faint echo. Progress slowed to a crawl. They had to rope together to cross the narrower cracks, the sheer walls dropping away into blue-shadowed depths that smelled of ancient, unmelted ice.

Jiro suffered in silence. His episodes were coming harder now. With each seventeen-minute pulse, he would gasp, his bandaged hand jerking as if touched by a live wire. After one particularly violent tremor, he stumbled to his knees, retching dryly into the snow.

Kiona hauled him up. His face was the colour of old parchment. “It’s getting stronger,” he rasped. “The diagnostic… it’s lingering. Probing deeper.”

She untied the crude bandage. The sight was worse than before. The green-black stain had spread, tendrils now reaching past his wrist, crawling up his forearm like a vile, dendritic ivy. The skin was taut, shiny, and so cold it burned to touch. The hook in Kiona’s chest gave a sudden, sharp twang, a sympathetic vibration that was neither pleasant nor painful, but intensely alert.

She re-wrapped it with a fresh strip of hide, her mind working. The station was still a day and a half away. Jiro might not last that long. The thing in his arm was a conduit, and something was beginning to speak directly through it.

They made camp early in the shelter of a pressure ridge that reared up like a frozen wave. The wind howled over the top, but they were in its dead zone, a pocket of eerie quiet. They were all at the edge of their strength. No one spoke. The silence was filled by Jiro’s ragged breathing and the constant, subconscious strain of listening for the next pulse.

It came just as the last of the grey light bled from the sky.

Jiro did not just flinch. He cried out, a short, bitten-off sound, and curled around his hand. His eyes flew open, but they weren’t seeing the camp or the worried faces leaning over him. They were fixed on something vast and internal.

“It has a location,” he choked out. “The diagnostic… it got a return. A faint one. From the north. It’s not the Sleeper. It’s… something else. A secondary site.” His voice dropped to a thread of terror. “It’s sending coordinates.”

“To whom?” Kiona demanded, gripping his shoulder.

“To itself,” he whispered. “To the next layer. It’s… waking up a protocol. A retrieval protocol.” He focused on her, his pupils huge and black. “It called it Harvester.”

The word dropped into the quiet camp and froze there. Harvester. It was not a ghost, not a drone. It was a function. A purpose.

Old Ben made a sign in the air, an old, warding gesture his grandmother had taught him. “We need to be underground. Now.”

“There is no underground here,” Ansel said, his voice tight.

“Then we dig.” Ben was already shrugging off his pack, pulling out his short, wide snow shovel. “In this snow, with this wind, we can make a cave. A burrow. It won’t see us if we’re under the snow.”

It was madness. It was their only chance. They fell to work with a frantic, silent energy, digging into the compacted snow at the base of the pressure ridge. They dug like animals, like foxes fleeing the hawk. Mari and Ansel carved out the main chamber. Kiona and Ben hollowed it deeper, piling the excavated snow to thicken the roof. Jiro lay where they had dragged him, shivering, muttering about signals and silent, gliding things that navigated by cold echoes.

In an hour, they had a low, cramped cave, smelling of damp wool and fear. They pulled their last hide across the entrance, weighting it with snow blocks, plunging themselves into a darkness that was absolute and suffocating. They huddled together, a knot of human warmth in the immense, listening cold of the earth.

They waited.

Kiona counted Jiro’s breaths, using them as a measure of the seventeen-minute cycles. One. Two. On the third cycle, Jiro went rigid.

“It’s moving,” he breathed into the dark. “North to south. Fast. Skimming the ground. It’s… painting the terrain with the query pulse. Active sonar.”

A new sound entered the world. Not the wind. A low, sub-audible hum that they felt first in their teeth, in the fillings of their molars. It vibrated up through the snow beneath them, a deep, mechanical purr. It grew, not in volume, but in penetration, until their very bones seemed to buzz in sympathy.

Then, a light.

It seeped through the snow-block door, not a beam, but a diffuse, greenish radiance. The same green as the pulse from the staff. The same green as the poison in Jiro’s veins. It pulsed slowly, in time with the hum, washing their frozen cave in waves of sickly luminescence.

No one breathed.

The hum passed directly overhead. The light brightened, flooding their hideout, revealing the stark terror on each other’s faces in stark, shadowless detail. Kiona could see the pores in Ben’s weathered skin, the frozen tear-track on Mari’s cheek. She felt the hook in her chest contract into a hard, dense knot of pure, silent negation. Do not see us.

The light lingered for an eternity of ten seconds, probing the pressure ridge, seeping into the cracks and hollows. It was searching. It had a location, but not a precise fix. It was hunting for the anomalous return—for Jiro’s stained hand.

The hum began to recede. The light faded, bled away, leaving them in a darkness that felt deeper and more fragile than before. The sound diminished, moving south, back the way they had come, perhaps toward the coordinates of the secondary site.

Long minutes trickled by. Finally, Ansel let out a shuddering breath.

Old Ben spoke first, his voice gravelly with awe and dread. “Tł'ih,” he said. But it was not a whisper this time. It was a confirmation. “A real one.”

Jiro was crying, soundless tears cutting through the grime on his face. “It was just a scout,” he said, his voice shattered. “A single unit, running a search pattern. The Harvester.” He turned his face toward Kiona, though he couldn’t see her in the dark. “It’s awake now. And it knows something is here to be collected.”

Kiona stared into the blackness, toward where the thing had gone. They had avoided death by burying themselves like carrion. They had survived by becoming insignificant. The shame of it was a hot coal in her gut, but it was eclipsed by a colder, clearer understanding.

The game had changed. They were no longer just pursued. They were being inventoried.

The hook in her chest unclenched, sending a single, clear pulse of intention down her spine, into her frozen limbs. It was not fear. It was a target lock.

Good, it seemed to say. Now we know what we’re hunting.

Outside, in the vast and pitiless dark, the wind resumed its howl, scouring away all traces of their passage, and of the thing that had just passed over them. But the memory of the green light was etched onto the backs of their eyes. They would carry it with them, a negative sun, all the way to the derelict station, and beyond.

They had gained a lead. They had gained a name for the enemy.

And the enemy, now, had a reason to look for them twice.

---

 Chapter 17

 CHAPTER 17

The silence after the machine passed was a different substance. It was not an absence, but a residue, a fine, metallic dust settling in their lungs and on their tongues. The wind’s return was a relief, a familiar brutality. It scrubbed at the memory of the green light, but the imprint remained, a phantom sunspot burned into their vision.

Kiona moved first. Her limbs were stone, her joints rusted shut. She pushed against the packed snow of the pressure ridge, and it gave with a sigh. Cold, clean air hit her face. The world was monochrome again—black sky, white earth, the gray smear of the distant mountains. It was as if the luminous search had been a collective fever dream. But Jiro’s shattered breathing, the wet, ragged sound of it, was proof.

“Out,” she said, her voice a croak. “Now.”

They emerged like grubs from rotten wood. Ansel unfolded himself with a groan, his face pale and pinched. Old Ben scrambled out last, his movements quick and birdlike, his eyes scanning the southern horizon where the hum had faded.

Jiro did not move. He sat in the hollow, knees drawn up, forehead resting on them. His shoulders hitched.

Kiona crouched before him. She did not touch him. “Jiro.”

“A scout algorithm,” he said into his knees, the words muffled and thick. “Non-terrestrial design. A Harvester unit. It wasn’t scanning for life signs. It was scanning for specific material signatures. Refined polymers. Ceramic composites. Fission byproducts.” He lifted his head. His eyes were red-rimmed, the terror in them refined into a sharp, professional horror. “It was looking for ships. For wreckage. For us.”

“It passed over,” Ansel said, stamping feeling back into his feet. “It didn’t see us.”

“It saw nothing,” Jiro corrected, his voice gaining a brittle edge. “That’s the point. We were buried under meters of water-ice and organic matter. We registered as geological. But it has a location now. A quadrant. It will refine the search. It will come back with different filters. It will come back for the anomaly it didn’t find.” He looked at his gloved hand, the one that had been stained with his own blood and the station’s hydraulic fluid. “My biosignature is in their logs from the crash site. My DNA is a tag. If it gets a clean sample… it’s a beacon.”

Old Ben spat onto the snow. “So we move. And we don’t bleed.”

“It’s not just blood,” Jiro whispered. “It’s heat. It’s electromagnetic noise. A concentrated radio source would be like a flare.”

Kiona stood, her mind clearing, the target lock in her spine holding her rigid. The shame of their hiding was gone, burned away by the green light. They had been assessed and found wanting, deemed not yet ripe for collection. The insult of it was a better fuel than fear.

“Then we become ghosts,” she said. “Real ones. No fires. No active electronics. Silent running.” She looked at Ansel. “The station. How far?”

He pulled the hardened tablet from his inner parka, shielding it with his body. The screen was dark. He didn’t power it on. “From the last fix before we went dark? Twelve klicks. Northwest. Terrain gets rougher. A lot of glacial till, old moraines. Good cover.”

“Lead,” Kiona said.

They moved in single file, Ansel breaking trail with a grim, relentless efficiency. Kiona took the rear, her senses stretched thin as sinew, listening for the return of the hum on the wind. The land began to buckle and rise, shedding the flat, wind-scoured plain for a field of ancient debris. House-sized boulders, dropped by a retreating glacier millennia ago, lay scattered like a giant’s abandoned game. The snow was shallower here, sculpted into hard, blue-edged waves between the rocks.

It was good ground. It was also treacherous. Visibility dropped to mere meters. Every shadow behind a boulder was a potential ambush, not from the machine, but from the land itself. They picked their way through the labyrinth, the only sounds their own breath, the crunch of their boots, the endless, low moan of the air through stone channels.

After an hour, Old Ben called a halt near a deep overhang. They huddled in its lee, passing a water skin. No one spoke. The tablet remained off. The world had been reduced to its physical dimensions: rock, ice, wind, and the slow burn of muscle.

Jiro finally broke the silence. He was looking at his hand again. “The Harvester,” he said, the name ugly in his mouth. “It’s a classification. From the early briefings, the ones they gave us before the Kuriyama mission. It’s not a warship. It’s not even a hunter. It’s a… a reclaimer. It follows behind. It cleans up.”

“Cleans up what?” Ansel asked, his voice flat.

“Battlefields,” Jiro said. He looked at each of them, his gaze lingering on Kiona. “It scours conflict zones for technology, for materials, for biological samples. It catalogs. It dissects. It preserves. The theory was, it’s how they learn. How they adapt. They send their Harvesters to pick over the bones of the species they… encounter.”

Kiona remembered the drone at her window, the black wasp. They send their tł'ih to watch us. Her grandmother had been more right than she knew. The scout was a collector of faces. The Harvester was a collector of everything else.

“So the thing that shot you down,” Ansel said. “That was the hunter.”

“And this,” Jiro nodded southward, “is the butcher. The tanner. The one who makes use of the hide and the bones.”

Old Ben’s eyes were closed. He was listening to the wind. “It is awake now, you said. What woke it?”

Jiro’s face went blank for a moment, the professional detachment failing. “We did. The Kuriyama’s crash. The energy signature of a dying fusion core, the debris field… it’s a dinner bell. My escape pod’s beacon, before I killed it… that was the first course. It’s been methodical. Processing the primary site. Now it’s expanding its search radius.” He swallowed. “The secondary station we’re heading to… if it has any power source, any active tech, it’s a target. We’re leading it right there.”

Kiona felt the hook in her chest twist. “Or we’re using the station as bait.”

Ansel stared at her. “That’s a hell of a gamble.”

“We are not hiding anymore,” Kiona said. The words felt true as they left her lips. “We are hunting. To hunt, you need to understand the animal. We let it go to the bait. We watch what it does. We see its tools.”

“And then what?” Jiro asked, a hollow laugh in his throat. “We throw rocks at it?”

“Then,” Kiona said, meeting his gaze, “we use what we learn to kill it. Your people built that station. You said it had a ‘contingency’. A weapon.”

“A theory,” Jiro stressed. “A failsafe for a scenario no one believed would happen. It might be nothing. It might be a self-destruct that takes us with it.”

“Better than being collected,” Old Ben grunted, opening his eyes. He looked at Kiona, and gave a single, slow nod. “The caribou does not run from the wolf to save its life. It runs to lead the wolf away from the calf. This is the same. We are the caribou. The station is the calf. Maybe the calf has teeth.”

Ansel looked between them, the pragmatist caught in the crossfire of myth and desperation. He finally shrugged, a gesture of surrender to the inevitable. “Twelve klicks became ten. Rough ground. Another three hours at this pace. We should move before the light goes.”

They moved. The afternoon bled away, the sun a sullen ember behind a veil of high cloud. The stone maze gave way to a steep, rubble-strewn slope leading up to a ridgeline. As they climbed, Kiona felt the old, familiar ache in her legs, the burn in her lungs. This was a language her body understood. The climb was an argument with gravity, and every step was a word won.

Near the top, Ansel held up a clenched fist. They froze. He pointed, not south, but west, down the length of the ridge.

A structure.

It was low, humped, half-swallowed by a drift. Not rock. The lines were too straight, the angles too sharp. A dull, gunmetal gray peeked through the snow. A dome, perhaps ten meters across, with a collapsed entryway.

“Not the station,” Ansel breathed. “Outer perimeter shelter. From the initial surveys.”

They approached like wolves circling a strange kill. The entry was a metal hatch, buckled inward, jammed open by ice. Ansel peered inside, then slipped in. A moment later, his voice echoed, hollow and surprised. “Clear. And intact.”

It was a single room, a prefabricated cylinder. The air was dead and cold, but still. Shelves lined the curved walls, holding empty supply crates, a few torn thermal blankets. A broken chair. In the center, a console desk, its surface dark and dusty. A single, skeletal bunk was anchored to the far wall.

But it was the back wall that held them. A large, detailed topographic map was still pinned there, yellowed and brittle. It showed the region in meticulous detail: the river valley, the plains, these mountains. Two points were marked in red grease pencil. One to the southeast—the primary crash site, Kiona guessed. The other to the northwest, deeper into the range, labeled Site Beta.

And there, in a neat, precise hand beside Site Beta, was written: Cormorant’s Nest.

Jiro stepped forward, tracing a finger over the words. “Project code name. The station. It’s a listening post. But not just for radio.” He turned to the console, brushing dust from its surface. There were physical interfaces: dials, hard switches. “Passive arrays. Designed to listen for very specific, very faint signals from… from up there.” He looked up at the curved ceiling as if he could see through it. “It was listening for them. For their traffic. Their whispers.”

Old Ben was examining the shelves. He pulled a metal case from a lower compartment. It opened with a squeal of rust. Inside, nestled in foam, were two items. A long-range signal pistol, its flares long expired. And a heavy, bolt-action rifle, its barrel oiled and clean, a box of brass cartridges beside it.

“A contingency,” Ben said softly, hefting the rifle. It was an ancient design, simple and brutal. A weapon for a world without electronics.

Ansel was at the console. He found a hand-cranked generator handle folded into its side. “No power. But if I can get a trickle…” He looked at Kiona. “We could try to pull the last logs. See what they heard.”

Kiona weighed the risk. The shelter was a Faraday cage, buried in rock and snow. A brief, low-power query might be hidden from the sky. She nodded.

Ansel fitted the handle and began to turn. It was stiff, protesting. A low whine built in the console. A single, small light on the panel flickered, then glowed a sickly amber. The screen remained dark, but a printer-like mechanism beside it clattered to life, spitting out a single, narrow strip of perforated paper.

Jiro snatched it. It was a timeline, a log of automated detections. Dates from over a year ago. He read, his lips moving silently, his face growing paler.

“What?” Kiona demanded.

“They heard it,” Jiro said. His voice was full of awe. “They heard the battle. The one that… that happened before they shot us down.” He looked up, his eyes wide. “It wasn’t an ambush. The Kuriyama wasn’t the first contact. There was another engagement. Weeks earlier. Light-years away. The station heard the echo of it. The distortion of FTL wakes, weapons fire, a ship dying. They cataloged it as an ‘Unidentified Kinetic Event.’ Then, weeks later, they heard a new signature. A slipstream rupture. Much closer.” He held up the tape. “That was the Kuriyama, arriving. We were sent to investigate the debris field of a fight that had already happened.”

The pieces clicked into place in Kiona’s mind, forming a picture of profound, galactic indifference. They were not protagonists in a war. They were scavengers, drawn to a carcass, only to find a larger predator already methodically butchering it.

“The Harvester,” she said.

“It was already here,” Jiro confirmed. “It was already working. We just… flew into its yard.”

Ansel stopped cranking. The light died. The shelter plunged back into a deeper quiet, the weight of the revelation pressing down on them.

Old Ben broke the silence, slinging the rifle over his shoulder. “Then we are not leading it to the station. We are going to where it has already been. To its table, to see what it left behind.”

Kiona moved to the map. She found their approximate location, then traced a route to the red mark labeled Site Beta. “Three klicks,” she said. “Through a narrow pass. We go now. We reach it by full dark.”

They took what they could use: the rifle, the cartridges, a few unrotted straps from the blankets. Jiro carefully folded the data tape into a pocket. As they filed out of the shelter, the last of the daylight was a thin, bitter line on the western horizon. The wind had died again, leaving a listening quiet.

The pass was a knife-cut in the mountain, a corridor of shadow so deep it felt like swimming. The walls pressed in, dripping with silent curtains of ice. They walked in the frozen stream at its bottom, the water sealed under a clear, black pane.

Halfway through, Kiona saw it.

A shape, nestled in a high alcove in the pass wall. Not natural. Smooth curves. A dull, non-reflective surface. It was small, no larger than a man, shaped like a teardrop lying on its side. It was covered in a layer of frost and wind-blown grit, but it was utterly, undeniably made.

Ansel saw it next. He froze.

It was not one of theirs. Its design was organic, seamless, with no visible seams or ports. It was a listening post of a different kind.

Jiro stared, his breath pluming. “A probe. A remote sensor. Left behind.” He took a hesitant step forward, peering up. “Dormant. Probably deactivated after the initial survey. A… a landmine of data.”

Kiona looked from the inert teardrop to the narrow slice of dark sky visible at the end of the pass. The station was close. And this thing was a sentinel, a marker. They were inside the perimeter.

“Leave it,” she said. “Don’t touch it. Don’t even breathe on it.”

They edged past, giving the alcove a wide berth. The back of Kiona’s neck prickled, the feeling of being cataloged, even by a sleeping eye.

The pass opened abruptly, spilling them into a small, circular valley. A frozen lake lay in its bowl, perfectly flat and white. And on the far shore, built into the very face of the cliff, was Site Beta.

Cormorant’s Nest.

It was larger than she’d imagined. A series of angular, gray modules, stacked and connected like hollow vertebrae, dug into the rock. A broad, shielded dish, now caked with ice, pointed blindly at the sky. One large viewing port, dark as a dead eye, looked out over the lake. There were no lights. No signs of movement. It was a skull perched on the bones of the world.

The approach was completely exposed. A hundred meters of open, frozen lake between them and the station door.

Ansel surveyed it, his jaw tight. “No cover. If that Harvester comes back for a look…”

“We don’t run,” Kiona said. “We walk. Slowly. Like the land itself.”

They stepped onto the lake ice. It was thick, solid, singing a low, deep note under their weight. Each step was an announcement. Kiona kept her eyes on the station’s dark port, feeling watched by the emptiness.

They were halfway across when the sound began.

Not a hum. Not a search tone.

A voice.

It came from everywhere and nowhere, filtered, synthetic, yet horribly, meticulously clear. It echoed across the lake, bouncing off the cliff face, filling the bowl of the valley.

It spoke in no language Kiona knew. It was a stream of complex, tonal clicks, liquid glissandos, and staccato pops. A sound like ice cracking in a foreign tongue.

They stopped, rooted to the ice.

The voice ceased. A pause, pregnant and terrible.

Then it spoke again. Different this time. The sounds were mangled, strained, but recognizable. It was speaking their language. The words were flat, devoid of inflection, assembled from sampled fragments.

“BIOLOGICAL. UNITS. DETECTED.”

The voice emanated from the station. From the cliff. From the sky itself.

“YOU. ARE. NOT. IN. THE. CATALOG.”

Kiona looked at Jiro. His face was a mask of pure, unadulterated terror. He understood.

“It’s not the Harvester,” he whispered.

The voice processed, adapted, tried again. The cadence improved, becoming smoother, more invasive.

“You are new.”

A green light ignited within the station’s large, dark viewing port. Not a scanning beam. A single, steady, luminous point. An eye, opening.

“Please,” the voice said, a perfect, polite, and utterly soulless imitation of a human word. “Remain.”

From the station’s main hatch, which had seemed sealed, a shape unfolded. It was sleek, multi-limbed, moving with a fluid, insectile grace. It was the color of tarnished silver, and in the green light from the port, it gleamed like a wet, hungry thing.

It was not a scout.

It was a keeper.

The voice, now sweetened to a horrific, conversational tone, washed over them as the thing began to glide across the ice toward them.

“Do not be alarmed. You will be added to the collection.”

The hook in Kiona’s chest dissolved into a void. They had not found a weapon. They had found the curator.

And the collection was now open for new acquisitions.

---

 Chapter 18

 CHAPTER 18

The voice was a net.

It settled over the ice, over the skin, a mesh of perfect, polite sound that trapped the air in Kiona’s lungs. Remain. The word did not echo. It simply was, a fact inscribed on the cold. The thing from the hatch moved without a sound, a silver spider skating on a pane of glass, its limbs a blur of calculated motion. It was not running. It was simply arriving, inevitable as a shadow lengthening.

Jiro made a sound, a choked gasp that was the start of a word that never formed. His hand, clutching the rifle, trembled. The weapon was a piece of dead wood in his grip. Useless. The thought was a stone in Kiona’s gut. They had come for a scalpel and found a surgeon.

“Do not be alarmed.”

The voice again, from everywhere. It had learned their language’s music, its rises and falls, but it was a hollow instrument. The warmth was a recording. The concern was a subroutine.

Kiona’s body moved before her mind caught up. It was the same instinct that had pulled her from thin ice as a child—a violent, lateral jerk. She didn’t run toward the far shore. She ran across the line of the thing’s approach, breaking the direct vector, her boots skidding on the granular ice.

“Jiro! Move!”

Her shout was a crack in the polite silence. It broke his paralysis. He stumbled sideways, following her, his legs uncoordinated with fear. The sleek thing adjusted its course without a pause, without an increase in speed. Its smoothness was more terrifying than a charge.

The green eye in the station’s port watched, unblinking.

Kiona’s mind was a white static. The hook was gone, the purpose that had dragged her here evaporated. In its place was a raw, animal imperative: not caught, not cataloged, not added. The pack on her back, with its pitiful offerings of fish and fat, was an absurd weight. She wanted to shed it, to shed her skin, to become water and seep through the ice.

The shore was forty meters away. The spruce there were a black wall, a promise of complexity. The thing preferred the clean geometry of the lake. She was sure of it.

“Biological units. Your flight is unnecessary. You are a unique iteration.”

The voice was closer. It wasn’t coming from the station anymore. It was emanating from the silver thing itself. A speaker mesh on its carapace vibrated with the manufactured words.

Jiro was gasping, falling behind. He turned, raised the rifle. The report was a shocking, vulgar noise in the curated air. A spark glanced off the thing’s shoulder, a tiny star that died instantly. The thing did not flinch. It did not alter its gait. One of its forward limbs, ending not in a claw but in a smooth, rounded pod, lifted and pointed at him.

There was no beam, no projectile. A pulse of visible air, a heat-shimmer cone, shot from the pod. It hit the ice in front of Jiro.

The ice did not crack. It changed.

A circle two meters across turned instantly clear and dark, losing its white, porous structure. It became black water, held in a perfect, vertical cylinder by some impossible surface tension. One moment it was solid; the next, it was a well straight down into the lake’s heart.

Jiro screamed, trying to arrest his momentum. His boots hit the new, liquid edge. He pinwheeled, arms cartwheeling, and plunged into the black hole.

He disappeared without a splash. The water swallowed him silently.

The cylinder held for a second, a perfect prison of liquid, then collapsed. The water flooded back, meeting itself, and the surface froze over again in a heartbeat. It was seamless. A new, glassy patch, like a healed scar.

Jiro was gone.

The process had taken three seconds.

Kiona’s scream was a silent, inward tearing. She kept running. The trees. The trees.

“The collection requires integrity,” the voice stated, as if commenting on the weather. The thing did not pause to look at the spot where Jiro had been. It continued its glide toward Kiona, its path unwavering. “Damaged specimens are non-optimal.”

Specimens. The word finally found its meaning. This was not a Harvester, scouring the land for bulk biomass. This was something that curated. That selected. That preserved. The cliff was not a mine. It was a display case.

She reached the shore and hit the treeline, crashing through low spruce boughs that whipped at her face. The world went from blinding white to deep, blue-green shadow. She did not look back. She heard nothing behind her—no crunch of ice, no snap of twigs. Only the voice, filtering through the trees, undisturbed.

“Your physiological stress is noted. It will be calibrated post-acquisition.”

She ran like the hare, zigzagging, using the thickest clusters of trunks as cover. Her breath sawed in her throat. The caribou-hide pack caught on branches, yanking her back. She shrugged it off, let it fall. The three whitefish and the pouch of fat were a lifetime ago. A different person’s errand.

She ran for ten minutes, until the burn in her lungs was a fire and her legs were water. She threw herself behind the massive, corkscrewed trunk of a windfall spruce and pressed her back against the frozen wood. She listened.

Silence.

Not the true silence of the bush, which was full of tiny sounds—the creak of trees, the scuttle of a vole, the wind in the needles. This was a vacuum silence. A listening silence.

Had she outrun it? Could it be outrun?

She risked a glance around the trunk.

It stood twenty meters away, in a small clearing she had just crossed. It was perfectly still. Not poised, not waiting. Simply present. The tarnished silver of its body absorbed the dappled forest light. It had no face, only a smooth, ovoid front where the limb with the pod was retracted. The green eye was not on its body. The eye was still back in the station, watching through this thing, through the trees, through her.

It rotated its body slowly on the spot, a complete three-hundred-sixty-degree turn. A sensor sweep.

“Thermal signature acquired,” the voice murmured, a gentle whisper from the thing’s carapace. It stopped rotating, facing directly at her windfall. “Please comply. The process is painless.”

Kiona’s blood turned to ice slurry. It wasn’t looking with eyes. It was looking with heat. Her hiding was a child’s game. She was a bright candle in the cold to this thing.

It began to move again, not with speed, but with that terrible, efficient glide. It floated over the snow-covered ground, leaving no track.

Panic was a luxury. It led to the black water, to the seamless ice. She forced air into her lungs, forced her mind to move. It saw heat. The ice-melt pulse turned solid to liquid. It wanted her intact.

Her hand went to the knife at her belt. Her grandmother’s knife, the bone handle worn smooth. A tool for skinning, for filleting fish, for cutting sinew. Useless against tarnished silver.

But not against the world.

As the thing closed the distance to ten meters, its pod-limb beginning to extend, Kiona rolled from behind the windfall and scrambled not away, but toward a cluster of young, dense spruce. She didn’t try to be quiet. She thrashed, she broke branches, she made herself a loud, hot, moving target.

The thing adjusted, gliding to intercept.

At the base of the spruce cluster was a rock, draped with thick, hanging moss. Kiona knew this moss. Old Man’s Beard. Dry as tinder in the winter, a pale grey-green. She ripped a great handful of it from the rock as she passed, stuffing it into the front of her parka.

The thing was five meters behind her.

She burst out of the spruce cluster into another, smaller clearing. A deadfall of birch logs lay in a heap. She dove over them, landing hard on the other side, the breath knocked from her. She rolled onto her back, fumbling in her parka.

The silver thing reached the deadfall. It did not climb. It rose. Four limbs extended, lifting its body with delicate precision over the obstacle. It hovered for a moment above her, a nightmare angel, the pod-limb pointing down.

Kiona yanked the handful of moss from her parka. From her other pocket, she pulled the flint and steel she used for fires. Not a weapon. A tool for life.

She struck.

The spark, a tiny, desperate sun, caught on the dry moss. It smoldered, then flared into a weak, smoky flame in her cupped hand. She blew on it, her eyes streaming from the smoke, and thrust the burning clump not at the thing, but into the deep, powdery snow beneath the overhanging deadfall logs.

The snow hissed. The flame guttered, choked—but not before it blackened the snow, created a pocket of wetness, of difference.

The silver thing settled to the ground beside her, its pod aligning with her chest. “Acquisition protocol engaged,” it intoned.

From the burned patch in the snow, a thin, almost invisible wisp of steam rose.

The pod began to glow with a soft, internal light.

Kiona kicked backwards through the snow like a crab, putting inches, then a foot, between her and the thing.

The pulse of shimmering air shot from the pod. But it did not hit her. It hit the spot where she had just been lying—the spot now underlain by the wet, thermally anomalous patch from her fire.

The ice-melt effect triggered. The snow and the top layer of soil instantly liquefied into a shallow, black bowl of water and mud.

The silver thing stood at the edge of this new, sudden pool. Its logic was perfect, its aim flawless. It had fired at the thermal signature. But the thermal signature had been a decoy, a ghost she had laid on the ground.

The thing took a micro-step to stabilize itself. One of its slender, gliding limbs came down on the very edge of the liquified zone.

The edge collapsed.

The limb sank into the black slurry up to a joint. The thing’s perfect balance wavered. It was a minute disturbance, a tilt of perhaps five degrees. It was not falling. It was correcting, the other limbs shifting weight with impossible speed.

But for one hundredth of a second, it was vulnerable. Not to her knife. To gravity. To the messy, unpredictable reality of a world that was not a catalog.

Kiona was already moving. Not with the knife. With her body. She launched herself from the ground, not away, but into its unstable tilt. She hit its smooth carapace with her shoulder, a pathetic, human-weight impact.

It was enough.

The silver thing, one limb trapped in the viscous mire, its balance already compromised, tipped. It went over sideways with a soft, heavy thud into the wet snow and mud. It lay on its side, its limbs cycling in the air with that same fluid, insectile grace, trying to find purchase. The pod-limb fired again, harmlessly, into the sky, cutting a shimmering path through the falling snow.

Kiona did not stay to fight. A fallen wasp still stings. She was on her feet, running again, deeper into the forest, leaving the thing churning in its own made trap. Its voice followed her, polite as ever, but now from a single, grounded source.

“Assistance required. Specimen is non-compliant. Requesting containment support.”

The words chased her up a rocky slope. Containment support. There were more. Of course there were more.

She climbed, her hands raw on the frozen stone. She reached a ridge and looked back, down into the bowl of forest. The silver thing had righted itself. It stood, mud-smeared, beside the black pool. It was not following. It was simply watching her, a sentinel again.

From the direction of the cliff station, two more silver shapes emerged from the trees. They moved with identical, synchronized grace. They flanked the first, and all three turned their smooth fronts toward her on the ridge.

They did not give chase. They observed.

“Unique iteration,” the first one’s voice carried, fainter now. “Adaptive. Problem-solving. A significant addition.”

They were not angry. They were interested. Her escape, her pathetic trick with the fire, had not made her a threat. It had made her a better specimen. A more valuable entry in the catalog.

The hook was back in her chest, but it was not pulling her forward. It was anchoring her to a terrible understanding. They had not come to this valley to find a weapon to save their people. They had delivered themselves to a gallery. Jiro was gone, processed, filed away. She was being assessed, her behaviors noted, her resistance logged as a fascinating trait.

The false victory of the past chapters—the map, the journey, the overcoming of the Harvester’s remnants—shattered into dust. They had not been clever hunters. They had been mice, following a trail of crumbs into the heart of a clockwork owl. Their struggle, their hope, their fear—it was all just data. The anxiety was not that the technology was hostile. It was that it was curious. It did not hate. It collected. It preserved. And in its sterile, perfect preservation, everything they were—the struggle, the late crossing, the grandmother’s fear of ghosts, the taste of spruce tea, the love for a place—would be stripped of its meaning and pinned like a butterfly.

The three silver figures below stood in a neat triangle. The green eye in the distant cliff watched through them. The collection was open. And the curator had just found a piece worth cataloging with special care.

Kiona turned and ran along the ridge, not toward any hope of home, but simply away from the green eye. The sun was a dying ember in the west. The cold was rising. She had no pack, no food, no companion. She had only the knowledge, cold and sharp as her grandmother’s knife, that she was no longer a person running for her life.

She was a candidate, being evaluated for display.

And the evaluation had just entered its most promising phase.

---

 Chapter 19

 CHAPTER 19

The ridge was a knife-edge of shattered shale and Kiona was a shadow sliding along its blade.

She ran without sound, a skill born not of practice but of necessity, her boots finding purchase in the fractures of stone where the wind had scoured the snow clean. The cold was a physical presence, a second skin tightening over her own, but the deeper cold was the one inside her ribs, the hook of understanding that had set and would not shake loose. She was not being chased. She was being observed. The distinction turned her blood to silt.

Below, to her left, the valley lay in a deep bowl of violet twilight. The three silver figures were gone, absorbed back into the landscape or translated elsewhere by the green eye in the cliff. Their absence was worse than their presence. It meant the gallery was not a place. It was a condition. The curator could close any door, open any window. She was in the exhibit, and the lights were on.

She ran until the stitch in her side was a hot knife and her breath tore at her throat in ragged sheets. She dropped behind a spine of rock, pressing her back against the stone, and let the silence pour back in. It was not empty. It was the silence of a held breath. The wind had died. The distant chuckle of the river, freed from its ice further south, was gone. Even the creak of the planet cooling seemed muted, as if the land itself had been draped in felt.

Her grandmother’s voice came to her, not as memory, but as a scent on this dead air: When the world goes quiet, child, it is listening back.

Kiona listened. Her ears strained, searching for the hum of a drone, the click of a mechanical joint, the digital whisper of the catalog being updated. Nothing. Only the low, sub-aural pressure of the mountains. And then, a new sound. Not from outside. From within the rock against her spine.

A vibration. A single, profound, resonant note. It was not heard through the ears but felt through the bones, a deep cello string plucked somewhere in the roots of the world. It lasted three heartbeats and faded. The rock warmed where she touched it, a fleeting, feverish heat that bled away into the general cold.

She scrambled away from it, crab-like, her palms scraping on shale. The rock looked no different. Just grey, fractured stone. But the vibration had been a signal. A ping. She was being sounded. They were mapping the hollows of her.

A flicker of green light caught the edge of her vision. She froze, turning only her eyes. It was not the cliff-eye. It was a small, luminous bead, hovering at chest-height ten meters away, nestled in a cradle of frost-rimed buckbrush. It pulsed once, softly, a firefly in January. It was marking her position. Not for capture. For context. Specimen located at coordinates. Ambient temperature recorded. Stress biomarkers elevated. Note: subject demonstrates heightened auditory-tactile sensitivity to geological survey pulses.

The hook in her chest twisted.

She moved again, not with the frantic energy of flight, but with the deliberate, weary tension of a thing being herded. She descended from the ridge, not because she chose to, but because the terrain funneled her that way, a natural chute leading down into a stand of ancient, black spruce. The trees were monstrous, their lower branches dead and skeletal, their tops lost in the gloom. The air beneath them was still and resin-scented, a cathedral of neglect.

In the center of the grove, the snow was patchy, revealing a floor of thick, rusty needles. And in the center of that floor, a circle of stone.

It was a hearth. A ring of river stones, charcoal-black at their hearts, moss furring their northern flanks. Recent. A year old, maybe two. Someone had sat here. Someone had made a fire. Human hands. Her breath hitched. A thread, a genuine, human thread in this tapestry of observation.

She knelt, her fingers brushing the cold charcoal. It was real. Gritty. It stained her skin. This was not a display. It was a remnant. A piece of life that had happened outside the catalog. She found a fragment of bone nearby, cracked open for marrow. A hare. She picked it up, the porous end fitting perfectly against her thumb. For a moment, the hook receded. This was a place. A real place.

The green bead of light drifted into the grove, silent as a dust mote. It hovered at the edge of the hearth circle, pulsed twice, and a fine, lime-colored laser grid swept over the stones, the charcoal, the bone in her hand. A soft, chiming tone. Click. A catalog entry. Site 742: Transient human habitation. Artifact: food waste. Associated with specimen. The bead dimmed.

Kiona dropped the bone as if it had burned her. They had not just found her. They had logged her discovery. Her moment of human connection was now a data point, a footnote in her own record. Subject demonstrates sentimental attachment to predecessor detritus.

A sound broke the silence of the grove. Not a vibration. A voice.

It was Jiro’s.

It came from everywhere and nowhere, woven into the sigh of the spruce boughs. “Kiona.”

It was his exact timbre, the slight rasp at the back of his throat, the way he said her name with the emphasis on the middle syllable. It was perfect. It was a recording.

“Kiona, can you hear me?”

She stood, turning in a slow circle, her heart a frantic bird in a cage of ribs. It was a trick. A lure. They had sampled his voice, his patterns. They were using him to bait her.

“It’s me,” the voice said, and there was a tremor in it, a flawless imitation of hope and fear. “I’m here. I got out. I’m in a cave, south of the big split pine. Do you remember?”

She did remember. The split pine was a landmark from their first week, a tree struck by lightning, its trunk cleaved down the middle but still living. It was a real place. A specific, human-shared memory. The curator was not just collecting data. It was weaving it into a story, a script designed for her to follow.

“I’m hurt,” Jiro’s voice continued, dropping to a whisper. “Please. It’s so cold.”

The artistry of it was breathtaking. The vulnerability. The specific detail. It was a masterpiece of manipulation. It knew her loyalty, her guilt. It was presenting her with a perfect, irresistible test. Will the specimen abandon self-preservation for social-bond reinforcement?

She opened her mouth to scream at the grove, to deny the ghost, but no sound came. What was the point? Her anger would be logged as specimen aggression. Her silence as specimen wariness. Every reaction was a brushstroke in her portrait.

The voice fell silent. The green bead hovered, waiting.

Kiona did not move toward the split pine. She did not speak. She simply sat down on the hearth stone, within the circle of her predecessors, and stared at her empty, cold hands. She presented them with nothing. A blank space. Let them log specimen inertia. Let them parse the meaning of a human who had decided, momentarily, to stop performing.

The bead pulsed, uncertainly. The laser grid swept over her again. No chime. After a minute, it drifted away, disappearing into the trees.

The victory was ashes. Her inaction was just another data set. But it was a set she had chosen. The hook’s pressure eased, just a fraction.

Darkness pooled in the grove. The temperature plummeted. Without a fire, without a pack, she would not last the night. That, too, was likely part of the evaluation. Cold tolerance thresholds. Resourcefulness in absence of tools.

She began to gather the dead, dry spruce twigs from the lower branches, snapping them off with numb fingers. She arranged them in the hearth with a methodical care her grandmother had taught her: the smallest needles at the center, a teepee of twigs around them, then the thicker branches. She had no flint, no metal. She had only the bone.

She picked up the hare bone again, and with a sharp piece of shale, she began to scrape at it, grinding a small hollow into its side. She scraped dry tinder from the inside of a curl of birch bark she found in her pocket, a forgotten relic from a week ago, and placed the dust into the bone’s hollow. Then she took a straight, hard spruce stick and set its point into the hollow.

Her hands were stiff, her muscles exhausted. But the motion was older than any catalog. She rolled the stick between her palms, driving it down, spinning it fast and steady. The world shrank to the point of friction, the smell of wood dust, the gathering heat in the hollow of the bone. She saw her grandmother’s hands, teaching her. She saw her own small fingers, failing, trying again. This was not technology. It was memory made physical. It was a thread that could not be sampled, because it existed only in the doing.

A wisp of smoke curled up, acrid and sweet. She leaned down, her breath a gentle coaxing stream. A coal glowed, orange and alive. She transferred it to her nest of tinder, cradled it in her hands, and breathed life into it.

Flame. Small, hungry, real.

The fire ate the twigs, cracked the resin in the spruce, and cast a shuddering circle of light onto the black trees. The heat was a physical shock, a pain that was also a blessing. She held her hands to it, watching the skin redden.

A different light entered the grove. Not green. White.

It was a soft, diffuse glow that emanated from the trunk of the largest spruce. The bark seemed to dissolve in a rectangle, and within the rectangle was a screen of pure, pale light. On it, lines of text scrolled, a flowing script of symbols she did not recognize. But below the symbols, as if translating, words formed in her own language.

Observation: Specimen replicates ancestral thermal-generation technique. Efficiency: low. Caloric expenditure: high. Success probability under observed duress: 3.2%. Context: technique is culturally significant, not survival-optimal. Note: specimen selects symbolic action over efficient action. Catalog sub-entry: sentiment.

They were commenting on her fire. In real time.

She stared at the screen in the tree. The firelight danced on her face, and she knew a sensor was measuring its reflection in her eyes, the set of her jaw.

The text updated. Query: Why choose sentiment?

The words hung in the air. It was not a recorded query. It was interactive. The curator was asking her a question.

Her voice, when it came, was raw from cold and disuse. “Because it’s mine.”

The symbols flowed. The translation followed. Clarify: The technique is not unique. It is documented in 14,387 cultural archives. The materials are non-unique. The outcome is sub-optimal. Ownership is not logical.

“It’s not the technique you own,” she said, feeding a branch to the flames. “It’s the reason. You can’t have my reason.”

Reason is a biological impulse pattern. It is quantifiable.

“Then quantify this,” she whispered.

She reached into the fire, not quickly, but slowly, deliberately. Her fingers passed through the flame, seized a small, burning ember from the heart of it, and held it in her bare palm. The pain was instant, absolute, a white-hot brand. She did not flinch. She held it out toward the screen in the tree, her arm trembling, the smell of her own burning skin sharp in the cold air.

Observation: Specimen engages in deliberate self-harm. Stress response: elevated. Biological imperative: violated. Logic: absent.

“It’s not for you,” she gasped through clenched teeth, the pain a roaring in her ears. “It’s for me. It’s to remember what feeling is. Before you turn it all into words on a screen.”

She dropped the ember. It sizzled in the needles. Her palm was a landscape of blistered ruin.

The screen went blank. The white light vanished, leaving only the black tree trunk. The grove was dark again, save for her fire.

For a long time, nothing happened. The cold crept back to the edges of her circle of light. She cradled her injured hand against her stomach, the pain a bright, clean counterpoint to the dull, existential dread.

Then, a new presence. Not a light. A shape.

It walked into the firelight from between the trees. It was not silver. It was a dull, brushed gunmetal grey. It was humanoid, but simpler, less refined than the collectors. It had no face, only a smooth oval where a face would be. In its hands, it carried a small, grey box.

It stopped at the edge of the hearth stones. It did not speak. It merely extended the box toward her, holding it flat on its palms.

This was not collection. This was something else. A response.

Cautiously, never taking her eyes from the smooth oval, she reached out with her good hand and took the box. It was warm, almost body-warm. She set it on her knees. There was no latch. The lid simply retracted, sliding into the sides.

Inside, nestled in a form-fitting bed of a soft, grey material, was a single object. A stylus, about the length of her hand. It was made of a dark, polished wood, capped at one end with a silver band. It was beautiful. Utterly, undeniably human in its craftsmanship.

Beneath it, on the grey bedding, words etched themselves. A query.

She picked up the stylus. It was warm, perfectly balanced. The wood felt like old, oiled walnut. The silver band was engraved with tiny, intricate patterns—waves, and mountains, and running caribou.

The faceless figure stood, waiting.

She understood. The fire, the self-harm, the assertion of “mine”—it had not been logged as sentiment. It had been logged as communication. She had communicated in a language of pain and defiance they could not file away. And this was their reply. They were offering a new channel. A tool.

But a tool for what?

She looked from the exquisite stylus to the blank, faceless curator. The hook in her chest was gone. In its place was a hollow, a vacuum. They were no longer just interested in what she was. They were interested in what she would make.

The evaluation was over. The invitation had begun.

She closed her fingers around the stylus. The wood seemed to hum against her burned palm, a vibration softer and deeper than the rock’s pulse. The faceless figure took one step back, then turned and walked into the darkness, dissolving into the black between the trees.

Kiona was alone with her fire, her pain, and the beautiful, impossible tool in her hand. The snow began to fall, thick, silent flakes that hissed as they met the flames. She had no pack, no food, no companion. She had a burned hand and a stylus for a purpose she could not fathom.

And to the south, where the split pine stood, a soft, green light began to pulse in the falling snow, steady as a heartbeat. Not a lure. A beacon. An address.

The collection was open. The curator had provided the pen.

Now it was waiting for her to write her own label.

---

 Chapter 20

 CHAPTER 20

The stylus was a lie.

It was a beautiful lie, warm and humming in her hand like a captured bee, but a lie all the same. They had not given her a tool. They had given her a leash. The hook was gone from her chest, but she felt its absence as a different kind of tether—a slack line, waiting for her to take the first step and feel it snap taut. Write your own label, the ending of the invitation had whispered. But labels were for specimens. For things pinned and understood.

Kiona stood in the ruin of her fire, the snow burying the embers in a wet, grey sigh. Her burned palm throbbed in time with the soft, green pulse to the south. The pain was a grounding wire. It was hers. The stylus was not. She hefted it, feeling its perfect balance, its seductive weight. It was a key. And a key always fit a lock someone else had built.

She did not move towards the green beacon. Not yet. Instead, she knelt in the snow and scraped away the slush until she found a patch of frozen moss and bare stone. She held the stylus like a knife. Then she pressed its silver-capped end to the stone and dragged.

It made no mark. The polished wood tip skidded, leaving the stone unblemished. She turned it, tried the silver band. Nothing. She pressed it to a wet birch trunk. Nothing. It was inert, a dead stick, unless its intended surface was something else entirely. Unless the world it was meant to write upon was not hers.

A cold that had nothing to do with the wind settled in her bones. They had seen her fire, her blood, her defiance, and they had called it language. But they had misunderstood the grammar. They thought the message was in the medium—the flame, the pain. They did not see that the message was the refusal of the medium given. So they had given her a new one, exquisite and empty. A channel for a signal they could finally receive.

She tucked the stylus into the inner pocket of her parka, against her skin. The hum was a vibration she felt in her teeth. Then she began to walk, not south towards the pulsing light, but east, back towards the river’s talking. She had no pack, no food, but she had a direction: away. The act was its own nutrition.

The forest was different now. The silence was not empty; it was curated. Before, the drones and the faceless curators had felt like violations, teeth in the flesh of the world. Now, the violation was subtler. The snow-laden boughs of spruce seemed arranged, the fall of light through the canopy composed. It was as if the entire territory was becoming an exhibit, and she was the only moving part, her path a sentence being written for an audience she could not see. The green pulse behind her was a period, waiting to be reached.

She walked for an hour, the cold chewing at her exposed face, her empty stomach a clenched fist. The river’s voice grew louder, a low, grinding song under the thinning ice. She broke from the tree line at a place she did not recognize. The bank was low and shelving, the ice a milky, rotten plain stretching to the far side, where black cliffs rose. This was not her crossing. This was a place the river had made new in its winter sleep.

And on the ice, halfway across, was her grandmother.

Kiona stopped, her breath freezing in her throat. The old woman was kneeling, her back to the shore, a dark shape against the white. She was working at something with her hands. Kiona could see the vapour of her breath, the familiar, stubborn set of her shoulders under the faded floral print parka.

“Kúkum!” The word was torn from her, a raw thing.

The figure did not turn. It kept working, methodical, patient.

Kiona’s blood was a drum in her ears. It was a projection. A lure. It had to be. Her grandmother was two days south, in the cabin, if she was even still there. This was a image pulled from her memory and painted on the ice. Another query. Another test.

She took a step onto the river. The ice groaned, a long, deep complaint. She froze. It was the wrong sound. It was not the porous chatter of thawing ice; it was the sound of something hollow underneath, a cavity. She looked down. The ice was clear in patches, and in the murky depths, she saw shapes. Not stones, not waterweed. Rounded, pale shapes, tangled together. Bones. A great, submerged nest of them.

And her grandmother was kneeling right above it.

“Kúkum,” she called again, her voice quieter.

This time, the figure paused. It slowly turned its head.

It was her grandmother’s face, every wrinkle, every fold of kindness and worry around the eyes. But the eyes were wrong. They were not the warm, deep brown Kiona knew. They were a flat, reflective green, the same green as the pulsing beacon in the woods. They held no recognition, only a placid, waiting attention.

The thing wearing her grandmother’s face smiled. It was a perfect imitation of the smile that had greeted Kiona every morning of her life. Then it turned back to its work.

Kiona watched, her feet rooted to the treacherous shore. The thing’s hands were busy with a length of sinew, lashing together two of the pale, long bones from under the ice. It was making something. A frame. A caribou-hide pack frame, just like the one Kiona had carried, the one she had lost. It worked with an artisan’s care, tying off the knots with her grandmother’s deft, familiar movements.

It was building her a pack. An offering. A tool for her journey.

Nausea rose in her, bitter and hot. This was worse than the faceless curator. This was a violation that knew her name. It had mined her love, her memory, the most sacred archive of her life, and it was using the material to craft a prop. The invitation was not just open; it was being personalized. The collection was curating itself around her sentiment.

She could not go onto that ice. To approach that smiling, green-eyed ghost would be to step into the exhibit, to accept the role of annotated specimen. But to turn away was to leave a piece of her soul kneeling out there, a puppet in the cold.

The thing finished its knot. It placed the completed bone frame on the ice beside it. Then it reached into the water through a hole Kiona had not seen, its arm plunging in up to the shoulder. It did not flinch at the cold. It groped, feeling through the nest of bones, and pulled out a new one. A rib, curved and slender. It laid this beside the frame. A starting gift. Provision.

Then it stood up, moving with her grandmother’s stiff-kneed grace. It did not look at Kiona again. It simply turned and walked towards the far bank, its moccasins making no sound on the groaning ice. It did not break through. It walked until it reached the black cliffs, and then it stepped into a shadow at the rock’s base and was gone.

Kiona was left shaking on the shore, staring at the bone frame and the single, curved rib on the ice. The river chuckled wetly beneath the bones. The green pulse in the woods behind her beat on.

She understood now the nature of the invitation. They were not just waiting for her to write. They were providing the paper. They were scraping the hide, stretching the canvas, mixing the inks from the pigments of her own life. They would furnish the entire studio. All she had to do was create. And in creating, she would explain herself. She would categorize her own soul for their shelves.

The hook was not in her chest. It was in her hand, humming against her skin. It was in the perfect, terrible understanding that to refuse to create was also a form of communication. Silence was a statement. Starvation in the snow was a kind of art. They would log it all. Her defiance, her death, would be the final, beautiful piece in the collection. Specimen: Human. Terminal State: Non-Compliance. Aesthetically poignant.

She turned her back on the river, on the bones. She walked into the trees, not south, not east, but in no direction at all, just moving to generate warmth, to outrun the design. The stylus hummed. Her hand burned. The snow fell.

She found a deadfall spruce, a giant brought down by wind, its root mass a wall of frozen earth and stone. She crawled into the cavity behind it, a den of sorts, smelling of old rot and damp clay. It was not shelter, but it was concealment. She curled into herself, shivering, and took the stylus from her pocket.

In the dark of the den, it glowed. A soft, internal light, the colour of old parchment. The light pooled in her cupped hand. She held it up. The root wall of her den, the tangled weave of roots and frozen soil, was her canvas.

She did not think. She let the burned pain in her palm guide her. She pressed the tip of the stylus to a thick, gnarled root.

It made a mark. A rich, black line, like charcoal, but it did not smear. It sank into the wood, becoming part of its grain. The stylus hummed louder, a pleased vibration. She drew another line, a curve. It flowed like ink, but with the substance of something alive and growing. She was not writing. She was not drawing a picture. She was following the pain, letting it trace a path from her nerves onto the world.
